<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Tram</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

</div>

<p>A <b>tram</b> (also known as <b>tramcar</b>; and in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> known as <b>streetcar</b>, <b>trolley</b> or <b>trolley car</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Railroad_car" title="Railroad car">rail vehicle</a> which runs on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_tracks" title="Rail tracks">tracks</a> along public urban streets, and also sometimes on a segregated <a href="/wiki/Right-of-way_(transportation)" title="Right-of-way (transportation)">right of way</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The lines or networks operated by tramcars are called <b>tramways</b>. Tramways powered by electricity, the most common type historically, were once called <b>electric street railways</b> (mainly in the USA). However, trams were widely used in urban areas before the universal adoption of <a href="/wiki/Electrification" title="Electrification">electrification</a>; other methods of powering trams are listed below under "History".</p>
<p>Tram lines may also run between cities and/or towns (for example, <a href="/wiki/Interurban" title="Interurban">interurbans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tram-train" title="Tram-train">tram-train</a>), and/or partially <a href="/wiki/Grade_separation" title="Grade separation">grade-separated</a> even in the cities (<a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">light rail</a>). Very occasionally, trams also carry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freight_(rail)" title="Freight (rail)">freight</a>. Tram vehicles are usually lighter and shorter than conventional <a href="/wiki/Train" title="Train">trains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">rapid transit</a> trains, but the size of trams (particularly light rail vehicles) is rapidly increasing. Some trams (for instance tram-trains) may also run on <a href="/wiki/Track_(rail_transport)" title="Track (rail transport)">ordinary railway tracks</a>, a tramway may be upgraded to a light rail or a rapid transit line, two urban tramways may be connected to an interurban, etc. For all these reasons, the differences between the various modes of rail transportation are often indistinct. In the United States, the term <i>tram</i> has sometimes been used for rubber-tired <a href="/wiki/Trackless_train" title="Trackless train">trackless trains</a>, which are not related to the other vehicles covered in this article.</p>
<p>Today, most trams use electrical power, usually fed by an overhead <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantograph_(rail)" title="Pantograph (rail)">pantograph</a>; in some cases by a sliding shoe on a <a href="/wiki/Third_rail" title="Third rail">third rail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trolley_pole" title="Trolley pole">trolley pole</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bow_collector" title="Bow collector">bow collector</a>. If necessary, they may have dual power systems — electricity in city streets, and diesel in more rural environments. Trams are now included in the wider term "<a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">light rail</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (May 2014)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> which also includes segregated systems.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_terminology">Etymology and terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The English terms <i>tram</i> and <i>tramway</i> are derived from the <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> word <i>tram</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title='"Please'>publisher missing</span></a></i>]</sup> referring respectively to a type of truck (<a href="/wiki/Goods_wagon" title="Goods wagon">goods wagon</a> or freight <a href="/wiki/Railroad_car" title="Railroad car">railroad car</a>) used in <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">coal mines</a> and the tracks on which they ran. The word <i>tram</i> probably derived from Middle Flemish <i>trame</i> ("beam, handle of a barrow, bar, rung"), a Romanesque word meaning the beam or shaft of a barrow or sledge, also the barrow itself. The identical word <i>la trame</i> with the meaning "crossbeam" is also used in the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French language</a>. Etymologists believe that the word <i>tram</i> refers to the wooden beams the railway tracks were initially made of before the railroad pioneers switched to the much more wear-resistant tracks made of iron and, later, steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The word <i>Tram-car</i> is attested from 1873.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the terms <i>tram</i> and <i>tramway</i> have been adopted by many languages, they are not used universally in English; North Americans prefer <i>streetcar</i>, <i>trolley</i>, or <i>trolleycar</i>. The term <i>streetcar</i> is first recorded in 1840, and originally referred to <a href="/wiki/Horsecar" title="Horsecar">horsecars</a>. When electrification came, Americans began to speak of <i>trolleycars</i> or later, <i>trolleys</i>. A widely held belief holds the word to derive from the <i>troller</i> (said to derive from the words <i>traveler</i> and <i>roller</i>), a four-wheeled device that was dragged along dual overhead wires by a cable that connected the troller to the top of the car and collected electrical power from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overhead_wire" title="Overhead wire">overhead wires</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portmanteau_word" title="Portmanteau word">portmanteau derivation</a> is, however, most likely <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a>. "Trolley" and variants refer to the verb <i>troll</i>, meaning 'roll' and probably derived from <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and cognate uses of the word were well established for handcarts and horse drayage, as well as for nautical uses.</p>
<p>The troller design frequently fell off the wires, and was soon replaced by other more reliable devices, the <a href="/wiki/Trolley_pole" title="Trolley pole">trolley pole</a> and notably the <a href="/wiki/Bow_collector" title="Bow collector">bow collector</a>. Both were fitted to the top of the car and were spring-loaded in order to keep, respectively, a small trolley wheel or grooved lubricated "skate" mounted at the top of the pole or a steel rod forming the top of the bow firmly in contact with the underside of the overhead wire. The terms <i>trolley pole</i> and <i>trolley wheel</i> both derive from the troller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Trams using trolley-pole current collection are normally powered through a single pole, with return current earthed through the steel wheels and rails, though some systems (e.g. parts of the London system), used twin overhead wires and poles to avoid the effects of stray currents flowing through the earth. Modern trams often have an overhead <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantograph_(rail)" title="Pantograph (rail)">pantograph</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_linkage" title="Mechanical linkage">mechanical linkage</a> to connect to power, abandoning the trolley pole altogether.</p>
<p>The alternative North American term <i>trolley</i> may strictly speaking be considered incorrect, as the term can also be applied to cable cars, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conduit_car" title="Conduit car">conduit cars</a> that instead draw power from an underground supply. Conventional diesel <a href="/wiki/Bus" title="Bus">tourist buses</a> decorated to look like streetcars are sometimes called <i>trolleys</i> in the US (<a href="/wiki/Tourist_trolley" title="Tourist trolley">tourist trolley</a>). Furthering confusion, the term <i>tram</i> has instead been applied to open-sided, low-speed <a href="/wiki/Trackless_train" title="Trackless train">segmented vehicles on rubber tires</a> generally used to ferry tourists short distances, for example on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Studios_Backlot_Tour" title="Universal Studios Backlot Tour">Universal Studios backlot tour</a> and, in many countries, as tourist transport to major destinations. The term may also apply to an aerial ropeway, e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Island_Tramway" title="Roosevelt Island Tramway">Roosevelt Island Tramway</a>.</p>
<p>Over time, the term <i>trolley</i> has fallen into informal use, and may be applied loosely to a wide variety of different vehicle types. The word has taken on a historic or picturesque connotation, and is often associated with tourist or leisure travel. In North America, professional or formal documents generally use more precise alternative terms, such as <i>streetcar</i> or <i>light rail vehicle</i> (LRV).</p>
<p>Although the use of the term <i>trolley</i> for tram was not adopted in Europe, the term was later associated with the <i><a href="/wiki/Trolleybus" title="Trolleybus">trolleybus</a></i>, a rubber-tyred vehicle running on hard pavement, which draws its power from pairs of overhead wires. These electric buses, which use twin trolley poles, are also called <i>trackless trolleys</i> (particularly in the northeastern US), or sometimes simply <i>trolleys</i> (in the UK, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_trams" title="History of trams">History of trams</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horse-drawn">Horse-drawn</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Horse-drawn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horsecar" title="Horsecar">Horsecar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Horsetrain_1870.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="331" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Horsetrain_1870.jpg/220px-Horsetrain_1870.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Horsetrain_1870.jpg/330px-Horsetrain_1870.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e9/Horsetrain_1870.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Horsetrain_1870.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Welsh</a> <a href="/wiki/Swansea_and_Mumbles_Railway" title="Swansea and Mumbles Railway">Swansea and Mumbles Railway</a> ran the world's first passenger tram service</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The very first tram was on the <a href="/wiki/Swansea_and_Mumbles_Railway" title="Swansea and Mumbles Railway">Swansea and Mumbles Railway</a> in south <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a>; it was horse-drawn at first, and later moved by steam and electric power. The Mumbles Railway Act was passed by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">British Parliament</a> in 1804, and the first tram (similar to streetcars in the US some 30 years later) started operating in 1807.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 230px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
<caption>External video</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=L7ILSgK4VgE" rel="nofollow"><span class="plainlinks">Clip from a Belfast horse tram in 1901</span></a> on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The first streetcars, also known as <a href="/wiki/Horsecar" title="Horsecar">horsecars</a> in North America, were built in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and developed from city <a href="/wiki/Stagecoach" title="Stagecoach">stagecoach</a> lines and <a href="/wiki/Bus" title="Bus">omnibus</a> lines that picked up and dropped off passengers on a regular route without the need to be pre-hired. These trams were an <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_railway&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Animal railway (page does not exist)">animal railway</a>, usually using teams of horses and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Mule" title="Mule">mules</a> to haul the cars, usually two as a team. Occasionally other animals were put to use, or humans in emergencies. The first streetcar line, developed by Irish born <a href="/wiki/John_Stephenson_(coachbuilder)" title="John Stephenson (coachbuilder)">John Stephenson</a>, was the <a href="/wiki/New_York_and_Harlem_Railroad" title="New York and Harlem Railroad">New York and Harlem Railroad</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_Avenue_Line_(Manhattan_surface)" title="Fourth Avenue Line (Manhattan surface)">Fourth Avenue Line</a> which ran along The <a href="/wiki/Bowery" title="Bowery">Bowery</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Fourth Avenue (Manhattan)">Fourth Avenue</a> in New York City. Service began in 1832. It was followed in 1835 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" title="New Orleans, Louisiana">New Orleans, Louisiana</a>, which has the oldest continuously operating street railway system in the world, according to the American Society of Mechanical Engineers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In other world regions, the first tramway systems (all horse-drawn) were:</p>
<ul>
<li>Continental Europe, 1839 - <a href="/wiki/List_of_town_tramway_systems_in_France" title="List of town tramway systems in France">MONTBRISON to MONTROND, France</a>;</li>
<li>South America, 1858 - <a href="/wiki/List_of_town_tramway_systems_in_Chile" title="List of town tramway systems in Chile">SANTIAGO, Chile</a>;</li>
<li>Africa, 1860 - <a href="/wiki/List_of_town_tramway_systems_in_Africa" title="List of town tramway systems in Africa">ALEXANDRIA, Egypt</a>.</li>
<li>Oceania, 1860 - <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Australia" title="Trams in Australia">SYDNEY, Australia</a>;</li>
<li>Asia, 1869 - <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Asia" title="Trams in Asia">BATAVIA (now Jakarta), Netherlands East Indies (now Indonesia)</a>;</li>
</ul>
<p>In many cases, these early forms of public transport developed out of industrial haulage routes or from the <a href="/wiki/Bus" title="Bus">omnibus</a> that first ran on public streets, using the newly invented iron or steel rail or 'tramway'. These were local versions of the stagecoach lines and picked up and dropped off passengers on a regular route, without the need to be pre-hired. Horsecars on tramlines were an improvement over the omnibus as the low rolling resistance of metal wheels on iron or steel rails (usually grooved from 1852 on), allowed the animals to haul a greater load for a given effort than the omnibus and gave a smoother ride. The horse-drawn streetcar combined the low cost, flexibility, and safety of animal power with the efficiency, smoothness, and all-weather capability of a railway.</p>
<p>In Australia, there were horse-drawn lines or systems in: <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Adelaide" title="Trams in Adelaide">Adelaide</a>, S.A.; <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Ballarat" title="Trams in Ballarat">Ballarat</a>, Victoria; <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Brisbane" title="Trams in Brisbane">Brisbane</a>, Queensland; <a href="/wiki/Gawler,_South_Australia" title="Gawler, South Australia">Gawler</a>, S.A.; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trams_in_Perth,_Western_Australia" title="Trams in Perth, Western Australia">Perth, W.A.</a>; <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>, N.S.W.; <a href="/wiki/Victor_Harbor_Horse_Drawn_Tram" title="Victor Harbor Horse Drawn Tram">Victor Harbor, S.A.</a> - the latter reconstructed and operating as a tourist/heritage line.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam">Steam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Steam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tram_engine" title="Tram engine">Tram engine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Casciano_in_Val_di_Pesa_-_Stazione_tranviaria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1554" data-file-width="2339" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/San_Casciano_in_Val_di_Pesa_-_Stazione_tranviaria.jpg/220px-San_Casciano_in_Val_di_Pesa_-_Stazione_tranviaria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/San_Casciano_in_Val_di_Pesa_-_Stazione_tranviaria.jpg/330px-San_Casciano_in_Val_di_Pesa_-_Stazione_tranviaria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/San_Casciano_in_Val_di_Pesa_-_Stazione_tranviaria.jpg/440px-San_Casciano_in_Val_di_Pesa_-_Stazione_tranviaria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Casciano_in_Val_di_Pesa_-_Stazione_tranviaria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Steam hauled tram in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> <i>c</i> 1890s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first mechanical trams were powered by <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam</a>. Generally, there were two types of steam tram. The first and most common had a small <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotive</a> (called a <a href="/wiki/Tram_engine" title="Tram engine">tram engine</a> in the UK) at the head of a line of one or more carriages, similar to a small train. Systems with such steam trams included <a href="/wiki/Christchurch" title="Christchurch">Christchurch</a>, New Zealand; Sydney, Australia; other city systems in <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>; <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>, Germany (from August 1883 on),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muenchen1964_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muenchen1964-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mohamedali_Tramways_Company" title="Mohamedali Tramways Company">British India (Pakistan)</a> (from 1885) and the Dublin &amp; Blessington Steam Tramway in Ireland. Steam tramways also were used on the suburban tramway lines around <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>; the last <i>Gamba de Legn</i> ("Peg-Leg") tramway ran on the Milan-<a href="/wiki/Magenta" title="Magenta">Magenta</a>-Castano Primo route in late 1958.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Tram engines usually had modifications to make them suitable for street running in residential areas. The wheels, and other moving parts of the machinery, were usually enclosed for safety reasons and to make the engines quieter. Measures were often taken to prevent the engines from emitting visible smoke or steam. Usually the engines used <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> rather than coal as fuel to avoid emitting smoke; <a href="/wiki/Condensing_steam_locomotive" title="Condensing steam locomotive">condensers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Superheater" title="Superheater">superheating</a> were used to avoid emitting visible steam.</p>
<p>The other style of steam tram had the steam engine in the body of the tram, referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Tram_engine" title="Tram engine">tram engine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Steam_dummy" title="Steam dummy">steam dummy</a>. The most notable system to adopt such trams was in Paris. French-designed steam trams also operated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rockhampton,_Queensland" title="Rockhampton, Queensland">Rockhampton</a>, in the Australian state of <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a> between 1909 and 1939. <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, Sweden, had a steam tram line at the island of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B6dermalm" title="Södermalm">Södermalm</a> between 1887 and 1901.</p>
<p>A major drawback of this style of tram was the limited space for the engine, so that these trams were usually underpowered.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cable-hauled">Cable-hauled</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cable-hauled">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cable_car_(railway)" title="Cable car (railway)">Cable car (railway)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:11_Cable_Car_on_Powell_St_crop,_SF,_CA,_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1138" data-file-width="1568" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/11_Cable_Car_on_Powell_St_crop%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg/220px-11_Cable_Car_on_Powell_St_crop%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/11_Cable_Car_on_Powell_St_crop%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg/330px-11_Cable_Car_on_Powell_St_crop%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/11_Cable_Car_on_Powell_St_crop%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg/440px-11_Cable_Car_on_Powell_St_crop%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:11_Cable_Car_on_Powell_St_crop,_SF,_CA,_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_cable_car_system" title="San Francisco cable car system">San Francisco cable car</a>: a cable pulled system, still operating as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The next motive system for trams was the cable car, which was pulled along a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_track" title="Rail track">fixed track</a> by a moving steel cable. The power to move the cable was normally provided at a "powerhouse" site a distance away from the actual vehicle.</p>
<p>The first practical cable car line was tested in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_cable_car_system" title="San Francisco cable car system">San Francisco</a>, in 1873. Part of its success is attributed to the development of an effective and reliable <a href="/wiki/Cable_grip" title="Cable grip">cable grip</a> mechanism, to grab and release the moving cable without damage. The second city to operate cable trams was <a href="/wiki/Dunedin_cable_tramway_system" title="Dunedin cable tramway system">Dunedin</a> in New Zealand, from 1881 to 1957. From 1885 to 1940, the city of <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_cable_tramway_system" title="Melbourne cable tramway system">Melbourne</a>, Victoria, Australia operated one of the largest cable systems in the world, at its peak running 592 trams on 75 kilometres (47 mi) of track. There were also two isolated cable lines in <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>, New South Wales, Australia; the North Sydney line from 1886 to 1900,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and the King Street line from 1892 to 1905.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> developed at least seven cable car lines.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (March 2013)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> A line in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_DC" title="Washington DC">Washington DC</a> ran to Georgetown (where some of the underground <a href="/wiki/Utility_tunnel" title="Utility tunnel">cable vaults</a> can still be seen today).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Los Angeles also had several cable car lines, including the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Second_Street_Cable_Railroad&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Second Street Cable Railroad (page does not exist)">Second Street Cable Railroad</a>, which operated from 1885 to 1889, and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Temple_Street_Cable_Railway&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Temple Street Cable Railway (page does not exist)">Temple Street Cable Railway</a>, which operated from 1886 to 1898. The most extensive cable system in the US was in <a href="/wiki/Chicago_City_Railway" title="Chicago City Railway">Chicago</a> between 1882 and 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (March 2013)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>, Germany, in 1901 an elevated <a href="/wiki/Suspension_railway" title="Suspension railway">suspended</a> cable car following the <i>Eugen Langen one-railed floating tram system</i> started operating. Cable cars operated on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highgate_Hill" title="Highgate Hill">Highgate Hill</a> in North London and <a href="/wiki/Kennington" title="Kennington">Kennington</a> to <a href="/wiki/Brixton" title="Brixton">Brixton</a> Hill In South London.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (March 2013)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> They also worked around "Upper Douglas" in the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> from 1897 to 1929 (cable car 72/73 is the sole survivor of the fleet).</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Melbourne_cable_tram_1905.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="752" data-file-width="1000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Melbourne_cable_tram_1905.jpg/220px-Melbourne_cable_tram_1905.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Melbourne_cable_tram_1905.jpg/330px-Melbourne_cable_tram_1905.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Melbourne_cable_tram_1905.jpg/440px-Melbourne_cable_tram_1905.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Melbourne_cable_tram_1905.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cable tram dummy and trailer on the <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_tram_route_96" title="Melbourne tram route 96">St. Kilda Line</a> in Melbourne in 1905.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cable cars suffered from high <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> costs, since an expensive system of <a href="/wiki/Cable" title="Cable">cables</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pulley" title="Pulley">pulleys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stationary_engine" title="Stationary engine">stationary engines</a> and lengthy underground vault structures beneath the rails had to be provided. They also required physical strength and skill to operate, and alert operators to avoid obstructions and other cable cars. The cable had to be disconnected ("dropped") at designated locations to allow the cars to coast by inertia, for example when crossing another cable line. The cable would then have to be "picked up" to resume progress, the whole operation requiring precise timing to avoid damage to the cable and the grip mechanism.</p>
<p>Breaks and frays in the cable, which occurred frequently, required the complete cessation of services over a cable route while the cable was repaired. Due to overall wear, the entire length of cable (typically several kilometres) would have to be replaced on a regular schedule. After the development of reliable electrically powered trams, the costly high-maintenance cable car systems were rapidly replaced in most locations.</p>
<p>Cable cars remained especially effective in hilly cities, since their nondriven wheels would not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_adhesion" title="Rail adhesion">lose traction</a> as they climbed or descended a steep hill. The moving cable would physically pull the car up the hill at a steady pace, unlike a low-powered steam or horse-drawn car. Cable cars do have wheel brakes and <a href="/wiki/Track_brake" title="Track brake">track brakes</a>, but the cable also helps restrain the car to going downhill at a constant speed. Performance in steep terrain partially explains the survival of cable cars in San Francisco. However, the extensive cable car system of Chicago operated over a large relatively flat area.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_cable_car_system" title="San Francisco cable car system">San Francisco cable cars</a>, though significantly reduced in number, continue to perform a regular transportation function, in addition to being a well-known <a href="/wiki/Tourist_attraction" title="Tourist attraction">tourist attraction</a>. A single cable line also survives in <a href="/wiki/Wellington" title="Wellington">Wellington</a>, New Zealand (rebuilt in 1979 as a <a href="/wiki/Funicular" title="Funicular">funicular</a> but still called the "<a href="/wiki/Wellington_Cable_Car" title="Wellington Cable Car">Wellington Cable Car</a>"). A third system, actually two separate cable lines with a shared power station in the middle, operates from the Welsh town of <a href="/wiki/Llandudno" title="Llandudno">Llandudno</a> up to the top of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Orme" title="Great Orme">Great Orme</a> hill in <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK" title="UK">UK</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_funicular_electric">Hybrid funicular electric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Hybrid funicular electric">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2e40x-020493santanastasio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="800" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/2e40x-020493santanastasio.jpg/220px-2e40x-020493santanastasio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/2e40x-020493santanastasio.jpg/330px-2e40x-020493santanastasio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/2e40x-020493santanastasio.jpg/440px-2e40x-020493santanastasio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2e40x-020493santanastasio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Former second generation cable tractor, used between 1978 and 2005, assisting a tramcar on the cable section of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opicina_Tramway" title="Opicina Tramway">Opicina Tramway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a>, Italy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opicina_Tramway" title="Opicina Tramway">Opicina Tramway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a> operates a hybrid funicular electric system. Conventional electric trams are operated in <a href="/wiki/Street_running" title="Street running">street running</a> and on <a href="/wiki/Reserved_track" title="Reserved track">reserved track</a> for most of their route. However, on one steep segment of track, they are assisted by cable tractors, which push the trams uphill and act as brakes for the downhill run. For safety, the cable tractors are always deployed on the downhill side of the tram vehicle. Similar systems were used elsewhere in the past, notably on the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Queen_Anne_Counterbalance&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Queen Anne Counterbalance (page does not exist)">Queen Anne Counterbalance</a> in <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Darling_Street" title="Darling Street">Darling Street</a> Wharf line in <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_.28trolley_cars.29">Electric (trolley cars)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Electric (trolley cars)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_electric_trams" title="History of electric trams">History of electric trams</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="1135" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg/220px-First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg/330px-First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg/440px-First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_electric_tram-_Siemens_1881_in_Lichterfelde.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lichterfelde tram, 1882</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Halle_ad_Saale_Strassenbahn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="682" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Halle_ad_Saale_Strassenbahn.jpg/220px-Halle_ad_Saale_Strassenbahn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Halle_ad_Saale_Strassenbahn.jpg/330px-Halle_ad_Saale_Strassenbahn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Halle_ad_Saale_Strassenbahn.jpg/440px-Halle_ad_Saale_Strassenbahn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Halle_ad_Saale_Strassenbahn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Historic German electric tram</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Electric trams were first experimentally installed in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, invented and tested by <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Pirotsky" title="Fyodor Pirotsky">Fyodor Pirotsky</a> as early as 1880.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> These trams, like virtually all others mentioned in this section, used either a trolley pole or a pantograph, to feed power from electric wires strung above the tram route. Nevertheless, there were early experiments with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storage_battery" title="Storage battery">battery-powered</a> trams but these appear to have all been unsuccessful. The first trams in <a href="/wiki/Bendigo" title="Bendigo">Bendigo</a>, Australia, in 1892, were battery-powered but within as little as three months they were replaced with horse-drawn trams. In New York City some minor lines also used storage batteries. Then, comparatively recently, during the 1950s, a longer battery-operated tramway line ran from Milan to <a href="/wiki/Bergamo" title="Bergamo">Bergamo</a>. In China there is a <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Nanjing" title="Trams in Nanjing">Nanjing battery Tram line</a> and has been running since 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first regular electric tram service using pantographs or trolley poles, the <a href="/wiki/Gross-Lichterfelde_Tramway" title="Gross-Lichterfelde Tramway">Gross-Lichterfelde Tramway</a>, was put into service in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin_Lichterfelde" title="Berlin Lichterfelde">Lichterfelde</a>, then a suburb of Berlin, (now part of the southwestern Berlin city district of Steglitz-Zehlendorf), by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siemens_%26_Halske_AG" title="Siemens &amp; Halske AG">Siemens &amp; Halske AG</a> (company founder <a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Siemens" title="Werner von Siemens">Werner von Siemens</a>), in May 1881. It initially drew current from the rails, with overhead wire being installed in 1883.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The company <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> still exists.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FortEdmontonParkTrolley2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1094" data-file-width="2048" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/FortEdmontonParkTrolley2009.jpg/220px-FortEdmontonParkTrolley2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/FortEdmontonParkTrolley2009.jpg/330px-FortEdmontonParkTrolley2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/FortEdmontonParkTrolley2009.jpg/440px-FortEdmontonParkTrolley2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FortEdmontonParkTrolley2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Edmonton Radial Railway Streetcar #42 in Fort Edmonton Park</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another was by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Joseph_Wright" title="John Joseph Wright">John Joseph Wright</a>, brother of the famous mining entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Whitaker_Wright" title="Whitaker Wright">Whitaker Wright</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> in 1883. Earlier installations proved difficult or unreliable. Siemens' line, for example, provided power through a live rail and a return rail, like a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Model_train" title="Model train">model train</a>, limiting the <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a> that could be used, and providing electric shocks to people and animals crossing the tracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Siemens later designed his own method of current collection, from an overhead wire, called the <a href="/wiki/Bow_collector" title="Bow collector">bow collector</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moedling_SB_Tw_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1751" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Moedling_SB_Tw_2.jpg/220px-Moedling_SB_Tw_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Moedling_SB_Tw_2.jpg/330px-Moedling_SB_Tw_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Moedling_SB_Tw_2.jpg/440px-Moedling_SB_Tw_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moedling_SB_Tw_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First type of Mödling and Hinterbrühl tramcars, bipolar overhead line</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1883, <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Volk" title="Magnus Volk">Magnus Volk</a> constructed his 2 feet (610 mm) gauge <a href="/wiki/Volk%27s_Electric_Railway" title="Volk's Electric Railway">Volk's Electric Railway</a> along the eastern seafront at <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">Brighton</a>, England. This two kilometer line, re-gauged to 2 feet 9 inches (840 mm) in 1884, remains in service to this day, and is the oldest operating electric tramway in the world. The first tram for permanent service with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overhead_lines" title="Overhead lines">overhead lines</a> was the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6dling_and_Hinterbr%C3%BChl_Tram" title="Mödling and Hinterbrühl Tram">Mödling and Hinterbrühl Tram</a> in Austria. It began operating in October 1883, but was closed in 1932.</p>
<p>Multiple functioning experimental electric trams were exhibited at the 1884 <a href="/wiki/World_Cotton_Centennial" title="World Cotton Centennial">World Cotton Centennial</a> World's Fair in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" title="New Orleans, Louisiana">New Orleans, Louisiana</a>, but they were not deemed good enough to replace the <a href="/wiki/Emile_Lamm" title="Emile Lamm">Lamm</a> fireless engines that then propelled the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Charles_Avenue_Streetcar" title="St. Charles Avenue Streetcar">St. Charles Avenue Streetcar</a> in that city.</p>
<p>In the United States, electric trams were first tested in service in <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a>, in 1888, in the <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Union_Passenger_Railway" title="Richmond Union Passenger Railway">Richmond Union Passenger Railway</a> built by <a href="/wiki/Frank_J._Sprague" title="Frank J. Sprague">Frank J. Sprague</a>, though the first commercial installation of an electric streetcar in the United States was built in 1884 in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> and operated for a period of one year by the East Cleveland Street Railway Company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:700_and_720_at_bispham.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1717" data-file-width="2400" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/700_and_720_at_bispham.jpg/220px-700_and_720_at_bispham.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/700_and_720_at_bispham.jpg/330px-700_and_720_at_bispham.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/700_and_720_at_bispham.jpg/440px-700_and_720_at_bispham.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:700_and_720_at_bispham.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Double-decker_tram" title="Double-decker tram">Double-decker tram</a> in <a href="/wiki/Blackpool" title="Blackpool">Blackpool</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first electric street tramway in Britain, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackpool_Tramway" title="Blackpool Tramway">Blackpool Tramway</a>, was opened on 29 September 1885 using conduit collection along Blackpool Promenade. Since the closure of the <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Corporation_Tramways" title="Glasgow Corporation Tramways">Glasgow Corporation Tramways</a> in 1962, this has been the only first-generation operational tramway in the UK, and is still in operation in a modernised form.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a> built a citywide system of electric trams in 1885.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/BKV" title="BKV">Budapest</a> established <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Budapest" title="Trams in Budapest">its tramway system</a> in 1887, and its ring line has grown to be the busiest tram line in Europe, with a tram running every 60 seconds at rush hour (however Istanbul's line T1, with a minimum headway of two minutes, probably carries more passengers – 265,000 per day). <a href="/wiki/Bucharest" title="Bucharest">Bucharest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgrade" title="Belgrade">Belgrade</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> ran a regular service from 1894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ljubljana" title="Ljubljana">Ljubljana</a> introduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ljubljana_tram_system" title="Ljubljana tram system">its tram system</a> in 1901 – it closed in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Australia there were electric systems in <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Sydney" title="Trams in Sydney">Sydney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Newcastle,_New_South_Wales" title="Trams in Newcastle, New South Wales">Newcastle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Geelong" title="Trams in Geelong">Geelong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ballarat" title="Ballarat">Ballarat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bendigo" title="Bendigo">Bendigo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Brisbane" title="Trams in Brisbane">Brisbane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Adelaide" title="Trams in Adelaide">Adelaide</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trams_in_Perth,_Western_Australia" title="Trams in Perth, Western Australia">Perth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fremantle" title="Fremantle">Fremantle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalgoorlie" title="Kalgoorlie">Kalgoorlie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonora,_Western_Australia" title="Leonora, Western Australia">Leonora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Hobart" title="Trams in Hobart">Hobart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Launceston,_Tasmania" title="Launceston, Tasmania">Launceston</a>. By the 1970s, the only tramway system remaining in Australia was the extensive <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Melbourne" title="Trams in Melbourne">Melbourne system</a> other than a few single lines remaining elsewhere: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glenelg_Tram" title="Glenelg Tram">Glenelg Tram</a>, connecting Adelaide to the beachside suburb of <a href="/wiki/Glenelg,_South_Australia" title="Glenelg, South Australia">Glenelg</a>, and tourist trams in the Victorian <a href="/wiki/Goldfields_region_of_Victoria" title="Goldfields region of Victoria">Goldfields</a> cities of <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Bendigo" title="Trams in Bendigo">Bendigo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Ballarat" title="Trams in Ballarat">Ballarat</a>. An unusual line that operated from 1889 to 1896 connected <a href="/wiki/Box_Hill,_Victoria" title="Box Hill, Victoria">Box Hill</a>, then an outer suburb of Melbourne, to <a href="/wiki/Doncaster,_Victoria" title="Doncaster, Victoria">Doncaster</a>, then a favoured picnic spot. In recent years the Melbourne system, generally recognised as one of the largest in the world, has been considerably moderrnised and expanded. The Adelaide line has also been extended to the Entertainment Centre, and there are plans to expand further.</p>
<p>In 1904 trams were put into operation in Hong Kong. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Tramway" title="Hong Kong Tramway">Hong Kong Tramway</a> is still in operation today and uses double-decker trams exclusively.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_trams">Gas trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Gas trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the late 19th and early 20th centuries a number of systems in various parts of the world employed trams powered by gas, <a href="/wiki/Naphtha" title="Naphtha">naphtha</a> gas or <a href="/wiki/Coal_gas" title="Coal gas">coal gas</a> in particular. Gas trams are known to have operated between <a href="/wiki/Alphington,_Victoria" title="Alphington, Victoria">Alphington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clifton_Hill,_Victoria" title="Clifton Hill, Victoria">Clifton Hill</a> in the northern suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, Australia (1886–1888); in Berlin and <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>, Germany; in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> (1920s–1930); between <a href="/wiki/Jelenia_G%C3%B3ra" title="Jelenia Góra">Jelenia Góra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jelenia_G%C3%B3ra" title="Jelenia Góra">Cieplice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sobiesz%C3%B3w" title="Sobieszów">Sobieszów</a> in Poland (from 1897); and in the UK at <a href="/wiki/Lytham_St_Annes" title="Lytham St Annes">Lytham St Annes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neath" title="Neath">Neath</a> (1896–1920), and <a href="/wiki/Trafford_Park" title="Trafford Park">Trafford Park</a>, Manchester (1897–1908).</p>
<p>On 29 December 1886 the Melbourne newspaper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Argus_(Australia)" title="The Argus (Australia)">The Argus</a> reprinted a report from the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Call" title="San Francisco Call">San Francisco Bulletin</a> that Mr Noble had demonstrated a new 'motor car' for tramways 'with success'. The tramcar 'exactly similar in size, shape, and capacity to a cable grip car' had the 'motive power' of gas 'with which the reservoir is to be charged once a day at power stations by means of a rubber hose'. The car also carried an electricity generator for 'lighting up the tram and also for driving the engine on steep grades and effecting a start'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Comparatively little has been published about gas trams. However, research on the subject was carried out for an article in the October 2011 edition of "The Times", the historical journal of the Australian Association of Timetable Collectors, now the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Timetable_Association&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Australian Timetable Association (page does not exist)">Australian Timetable Association</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceti_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ceti-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>A tram system powered by <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> was due to open in <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NgvJournal_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-NgvJournal-30">[30]</a></sup> but as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> there was no evidence of anything having happened; news about the project appears to have dried up.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_power_sources">Other power sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Other power sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bensinmotorvagn._Trafikerade_linjen_19,_Karlaplan_-_Frihamnen_1924_-_1929.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="484" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bensinmotorvagn._Trafikerade_linjen_19%2C_Karlaplan_-_Frihamnen_1924_-_1929.JPG/220px-Bensinmotorvagn._Trafikerade_linjen_19%2C_Karlaplan_-_Frihamnen_1924_-_1929.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bensinmotorvagn._Trafikerade_linjen_19%2C_Karlaplan_-_Frihamnen_1924_-_1929.JPG/330px-Bensinmotorvagn._Trafikerade_linjen_19%2C_Karlaplan_-_Frihamnen_1924_-_1929.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bensinmotorvagn._Trafikerade_linjen_19%2C_Karlaplan_-_Frihamnen_1924_-_1929.JPG/440px-Bensinmotorvagn._Trafikerade_linjen_19%2C_Karlaplan_-_Frihamnen_1924_-_1929.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bensinmotorvagn._Trafikerade_linjen_19,_Karlaplan_-_Frihamnen_1924_-_1929.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The only petrol-driven tram of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stockholms_Sp%C3%A5rv%C3%A4gar" title="Stockholms Spårvägar">Stockholms Spårvägar</a>, on line 19 in the 1920s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In some places, other forms of power were used to power the tram. <a href="/wiki/Hastings" title="Hastings">Hastings</a> and some other tramways, for example <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stockholms_Sp%C3%A5rv%C3%A4gar" title="Stockholms Spårvägar">Stockholms Spårvägar</a> in Sweden and some lines in <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a>, used <a href="/wiki/Petrol_engine" title="Petrol engine">petrol</a> trams. Paris operated trams that were powered by <a href="/wiki/Pneumatics" title="Pneumatics">compressed air</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Mekarski_system" title="Mekarski system">Mekarski system</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Galveston_Island_Trolley" title="Galveston Island Trolley">Galveston Island Trolley</a> in Texas operated <a href="/wiki/Galveston_Island_Trolley" title="Galveston Island Trolley">diesel trams</a> due to the city's hurricane-prone location, which would result in frequent damage to an electrical supply system.</p>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Victoria" title="Portland, Victoria">Portland, Victoria</a> promotes its tourist tram<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> as being a cable car it actually operates using a hidden diesel motor. The tram, which runs on a circular route around the town of Portland, uses 'dummies' (grip-cars) and saloon trailer cars formerly used on the extensive <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_cable_tramway_system" title="Melbourne cable tramway system">Melbourne cable tramway system</a> and now beautifully restored.</p>
<p>In March 2015, <a href="/wiki/CSR_Corporation_Limited" title="CSR Corporation Limited">China South Rail Corporation</a> (CSR) demonstrated the world's first hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell_vehicle" title="Fuel cell vehicle">fuel cell vehicle</a> tramcar at an assembly facility in Qingdao. The chief engineer of the CSR subsidiary <a href="/wiki/CSR_Sifang_Co_Ltd." title="CSR Sifang Co Ltd.">CSR Sifang Co Ltd.</a>, Liang Jianying, said that the company is studying how to reduce the running costs of the tram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Single-ended_vs_double-ended">Single-ended vs double-ended</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Single-ended vs double-ended">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tram_in_Warsaw.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3846" data-file-width="2885" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tram_in_Warsaw.jpg/220px-Tram_in_Warsaw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tram_in_Warsaw.jpg/330px-Tram_in_Warsaw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tram_in_Warsaw.jpg/440px-Tram_in_Warsaw.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tram_in_Warsaw.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A tram with double-ended features in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A double-ended tram has an operator's cab and controls at each end of the vehicle, which allows it to easily be driven at full speed in either direction on a continuous segment of track. Typically at the end of a run, the tram's operator will walk from one end of the tram to the other, and then commence the tram route in the other direction. The tram is usually switched to another track by use of <a href="/wiki/Railroad_switch" title="Railroad switch">crossover points</a> or Y-points. Conversely, a single-ended vehicle needs a method of turning at termini so that the operator's cab is in the front of the tram for the reverse journey. This usually necessitates a turning loop or triangle. On the other hand, the single cab and controls and fewer door spaces make the tram lighter, increases passenger accommodation (including many more seats) and effects reductions in equipment, weight, first-cost, maintenance cost, and operating expense.</p>
<p>A single-ended tram has operator's controls at only one end, and can safely be driven at speed in the forward direction but is also capable of reverse movement, typically at slower speed, using a small set of controls at the rear. The configuration of the doors is usually asymmetrical, favouring the side expected to be closest to the street <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerb" title="Kerb">kerb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Footpath" title="Footpath">footpath</a>. At the end of a run, the tram must be turned around via a <a href="/wiki/Balloon_loop" title="Balloon loop">balloon loop</a> or some other method, to face in the opposite direction for a return trip.</p>
<p>Two single-ended trams with doors on both sides may be coupled into a (semi-)permanently coupled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Married_pair" title="Married pair">married pair</a> or <a href="/wiki/Twin_unit" title="Twin unit">twinset</a>, with operator's controls at each end of the combination. Such a setup is operated as if it were a double-ended tram, except that the operator must exit one vehicle and enter the other, when reversing at the end of the run.</p>
<p>In addition, if overhead electrical power is fed from a <a href="/wiki/Trolley_pole" title="Trolley pole">trolley pole</a>, the direction of the trolley pole must be reversed at the end of the run, to ensure that the pole is "pulled" behind or "trailing" the vehicle, to avoid 'dewiring'. This was achieved by a member of the crew swinging the pole through 180 degrees (if there was only one pole) or lowering one pole and raising the other if there were two. More commonly nowadays, a bidirectional <a href="/wiki/Pantograph_(transport)" title="Pantograph (transport)">pantograph</a> may be used to feed power, eliminating the need for an extra procedure when reversing direction.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low_floor">Low floor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Low floor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Low-floor_tram" title="Low-floor tram">Low-floor tram</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Braunschweiger_Trams.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Braunschweiger_Trams.jpg/220px-Braunschweiger_Trams.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Braunschweiger_Trams.jpg/330px-Braunschweiger_Trams.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Braunschweiger_Trams.jpg/440px-Braunschweiger_Trams.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Braunschweiger_Trams.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Two Trams in Braunschweig, Germany. The left one is a 1981 high-floor tram, the right one a 2007 low-floor</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From around the 1990s, light rail vehicles not made for the occasional high platform light rail system have usually been of partial or fully low-floor design, with the floor 300 to 360 mm (11.8 to 14.2 in) above top of rail, a capability not found in older vehicles. This allows them to load passengers, including those in <a href="/wiki/Wheelchair" title="Wheelchair">wheelchairs</a>, directly from low-rise platforms that are not much more than raised footpaths/sidewalks. This satisfies requirements to provide access to disabled passengers without using expensive <a href="/wiki/Wheelchair_lift" title="Wheelchair lift">wheelchair lifts</a>, while at the same time making boarding faster and easier for other passengers.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hrad%C4%8Dansk%C3%A1,_9226.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2488" data-file-width="3318" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Hrad%C4%8Dansk%C3%A1%2C_9226.jpg/220px-Hrad%C4%8Dansk%C3%A1%2C_9226.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Hrad%C4%8Dansk%C3%A1%2C_9226.jpg/330px-Hrad%C4%8Dansk%C3%A1%2C_9226.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Hrad%C4%8Dansk%C3%A1%2C_9226.jpg/440px-Hrad%C4%8Dansk%C3%A1%2C_9226.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hrad%C4%8Dansk%C3%A1,_9226.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Entirely low-floor <a href="/wiki/%C5%A0koda_15_T" title="Škoda 15 T">Škoda ForCity</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prague_tram_system" title="Prague tram system">Prague</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Various companies have developed particular low-floor designs, varying from part-low-floor (with internal steps between the low-floor section and the high-floor sections over the bogies), e.g. Citytram<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Siemens_S70" title="Siemens S70">Siemens S70</a>, to 100% low-floor, where the floor passes through a corridor between the drive wheels, thus maintaining a relatively constant (stepless) level from end to end of the tram.</p>
<p>Prior to the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/%C5%A0koda_15_T" title="Škoda 15 T">Škoda ForCity</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> this carried the mechanical penalty of requiring bogies to be fixed and unable to pivot (except for less than 5 degrees in some trams) and thus reducing <a href="/wiki/Minimum_railway_curve_radius" title="Minimum railway curve radius">curve negotiation</a>. This creates undue wear on the tracks and wheels.</p>
<p>Passengers appreciate the ease of boarding and alighting from low-floor trams and moving about inside 100% low-floor trams. Passenger satisfaction with low-floor trams is high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yarra_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yarra-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Low-floor trams are now running in many cities around the world, including <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">Bratislava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gold_Coast,_Australia" title="Gold Coast, Australia">Gold Coast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, Milan, <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sydney,_Australia" title="Sydney, Australia">Sydney</a>, Vienna, <a href="/wiki/Zagreb" title="Zagreb">Zagreb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electron_T5L64_Franka_square_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2089" data-file-width="2769" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Electron_T5L64_Franka_square_1.JPG/220px-Electron_T5L64_Franka_square_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Electron_T5L64_Franka_square_1.JPG/330px-Electron_T5L64_Franka_square_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Electron_T5L64_Franka_square_1.JPG/440px-Electron_T5L64_Franka_square_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electron_T5L64_Franka_square_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Low-floor tram on the streets of <a href="/wiki/Lviv" title="Lviv">Lviv</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultra_low_floor">Ultra low floor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ultra low floor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ultra_Low_Floor" title="Ultra Low Floor">Ultra Low Floor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ULF_B.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1389" data-file-width="2109" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/ULF_B.jpg/220px-ULF_B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/ULF_B.jpg/330px-ULF_B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/ULF_B.jpg/440px-ULF_B.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ULF_B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A type B ULF tram in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Ultra Low Floor or (ULF) tram is a type of low-floor tram operating in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> as of 1997 and in <a href="/wiki/Oradea" title="Oradea">Oradea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, with the lowest floor-height of any such vehicle. In contrast to other low-floor trams, the floor in the interior of ULF is at sidewalk height (about 18 cm or 7 inches above the road surface), which makes access to trams easy for passengers in wheelchairs or with baby carriages. This configuration required a new undercarriage. The axles had to be replaced by a complicated electronic steering of the <a href="/wiki/Traction_motor" title="Traction motor">traction motors</a>. Auxiliary devices are installed largely under the car's roof.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Articulated">Articulated</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Articulated">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Combino_Supra_2036_in_Budapest.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="779" data-file-width="2193" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Combino_Supra_2036_in_Budapest.JPG/220px-Combino_Supra_2036_in_Budapest.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Combino_Supra_2036_in_Budapest.JPG/330px-Combino_Supra_2036_in_Budapest.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Combino_Supra_2036_in_Budapest.JPG/440px-Combino_Supra_2036_in_Budapest.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Combino_Supra_2036_in_Budapest.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combino_Supra" title="Combino Supra">Combino Supra</a> articulated tram in <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Articulated_vehicle" title="Articulated vehicle">Articulated</a> trams, invented and first used by the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Elevated_Railway" title="Boston Elevated Railway">Boston Elevated Railway</a> in 1912–13<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> at a total length of about twelve meters long (40 ft) for each pioneering example of twin-section articulated tram car, have two or more body sections, connected by flexible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joint_(mechanics)" title="Joint (mechanics)">joints</a> and a round platform at their pivoting midsection(s). Like <a href="/wiki/Articulated_bus" title="Articulated bus">articulated buses</a>, they have increased passenger capacity. In practice, these trams can be up to 56 metres (184 ft) long (such as <a href="/wiki/Urbos" title="Urbos">CAF Urbos 3</a> in Budapest, Hungary), while a regular tram has to be much shorter. With this type, the articulation is normally suspended between carbody sections.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/%C5%A0koda_15_T" title="Škoda 15 T">Škoda ForCity</a>, which is the world's first 100% low floor tram with pivoting bogies, a <a href="/wiki/Jacobs_bogie" title="Jacobs bogie">Jacobs bogie</a> supports the articulation between the two or more carbody sections. An articulated tram may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_floor" title="Low floor">low-floor</a> variety or high (regular) floor variety. Newer model trams may be up to 72 metres (236 ft) long and carry 510 passengers at a comfortable <span class="nowrap">4 passengers/m<sup>2</sup></span>. At <a href="/wiki/Crush_load" title="Crush load">crush loadings</a> this would be even higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Double_decker">Double decker</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Double decker">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Double-decker_tram" title="Double-decker tram">Double-decker tram</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tram_in_hanwell_boston_road.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3560" data-file-width="5904" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Tram_in_hanwell_boston_road.JPG/220px-Tram_in_hanwell_boston_road.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Tram_in_hanwell_boston_road.JPG/330px-Tram_in_hanwell_boston_road.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Tram_in_hanwell_boston_road.JPG/440px-Tram_in_hanwell_boston_road.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tram_in_hanwell_boston_road.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A London United Tramways double deck tram in 1910</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Double decker trams were commonplace in Great Britain and <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> Ireland before most tramways were torn up in the 1950s and 1960s.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Railways" title="New York Railways">New York Railways</a> experimented in 1912 with a <a href="/wiki/J._G._Brill_Company" title="J. G. Brill Company">Brill</a> double deck Hedley-Doyle stepless center entrance car, nicknamed the "Broadway Battleship," a term that spread to other large streetcars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bain_Smithsonian_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bain_Smithsonian-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hobart" title="Hobart">Hobart</a>, Tasmania, Australia made extensive use of double decker trams. Arguably the most unusual double-decker tram used to run between the isolated Western Australian <a href="/wiki/Outback" title="Outback">outback</a> town of <a href="/wiki/Leonora,_Western_Australia" title="Leonora, Western Australia">Leonora</a> and the nearby settlement of <a href="/wiki/Gwalia,_Western_Australia" title="Gwalia, Western Australia">Gwalia</a>.</p>
<p>Double decker trams still operate in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandria_Tram" title="Alexandria Tram">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blackpool_tramway" title="Blackpool tramway">Blackpool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Tramways" title="Hong Kong Tramways">Hong Kong</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tram-train">Tram-train</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Tram-train">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tram-train" title="Tram-train">Tram-train</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tram-train" title="Tram-train">Tram-train</a> operation uses vehicles such as the <a href="/wiki/Flexity_Link" title="Flexity Link">Flexity Link</a> and Regio-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citadis" title="Citadis">Citadis</a>, which are suited for use on urban tram lines and also meet the necessary indication, power, and strength requirements for operation on main-line railways. This allows passengers to travel from suburban areas into city-centre destinations without having to change from a train to a tram.</p>
<p>It has been primarily developed in Germanic countries, in particular Germany and Switzerland. <a href="/wiki/Karlsruhe_Stadtbahn" title="Karlsruhe Stadtbahn">Karlsruhe</a> is a notable pioneer of the tram-train.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-commuter">Non-commuter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Non-commuter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cargo_trams">Cargo trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Cargo trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VW-Cargotram-Dresden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="838" data-file-width="1064" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/VW-Cargotram-Dresden.jpg/220px-VW-Cargotram-Dresden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/VW-Cargotram-Dresden.jpg/330px-VW-Cargotram-Dresden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/VW-Cargotram-Dresden.jpg/440px-VW-Cargotram-Dresden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VW-Cargotram-Dresden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/CarGoTram" title="CarGoTram">CarGoTram</a> run by <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>, Germany on a section of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grassed_track" title="Grassed track">grassed track</a>. It delivers parts to the <a href="/wiki/Transparent_Factory" title="Transparent Factory">Transparent Factory</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
