<p>Since the 19th century goods have been carried on rail vehicles through the streets, often near docks and steelworks, for example the <a href="/wiki/Weymouth_Harbour_Tramway" title="Weymouth Harbour Tramway">Weymouth Harbour Tramway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Weymouth,_Dorset" title="Weymouth, Dorset">Weymouth, Dorset</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Belgian <a href="/wiki/Vicinal_tramway" title="Vicinal tramway">vicinal tramway</a> routes were used to haul agricultural produce, timber and coal from Blégny colliery while several of the US interurbans carried freight. In Australia, three different "Freight Cars" operated in <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> between 1927 and 1977<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-40">[40]</a></sup> and the city of <a href="/wiki/Kislovodsk" title="Kislovodsk">Kislovodsk</a> in Russia had a freight-only tram system consisting of one line which was used exclusively to deliver bottled Narzan mineral water to the railway station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today, the German city of <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a> has a regular <i><a href="/wiki/CarGoTram" title="CarGoTram">CarGoTram</a></i> service, run by the world's longest tram trainsets (59.4 metres (195 ft)), carrying car parts across the city centre to its <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> factory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In addition to Dresden, the cities of <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a> currently use trams as mobile recycling depots<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p>At the turn of the 21st century, a new interest has arisen in using urban tramway systems to transport goods. The motivation now is to reduce air pollution, traffic congestion and damage to road surfaces in city centres.</p>
<p>One recent proposal to bring cargo tramways back into wider use was the plan by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=City_Cargo_Amsterdam&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="City Cargo Amsterdam (page does not exist)">City Cargo Amsterdam</a> to reintroduce them into the city of <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>. In the spring of 2007 the city piloted this cargo tram operation, which among its aims aimed to reduce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">particulate</a> pollution in the city by 20% by halving the number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorries" title="Lorries">lorries</a> (5,000) unloading in the <a href="/wiki/Inner_city" title="Inner city">inner city</a> during the permitted timeframe from 07:00 till 10:30. The pilot involved two cargo trams, operating from a distribution centre and delivering to a "hub" where special electric trucks delivered the trams' small <a href="/wiki/Shipping_container" title="Shipping container">containers</a> to their final destination. The trial was successful, releasing an intended investment of €100 million in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fleet_vehicles" title="Fleet vehicles">fleet</a> of 52 cargo trams distributing from four peripheral "cross docks" to 15 inner-city hubs by 2012. These specially built vehicles would be 30 feet (9.14 m) long with 12 <a href="/wiki/Axle" title="Axle">axles</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Cargo" title="Cargo">payload</a> of 30 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (33.1 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>; 29.5 <a href="/wiki/Long_ton" title="Long ton">long tons</a>). On weekdays, trams are planned to make 4 deliveries per hour between 7 a.m. and 11 a.m. and two per hour between 11 a.m. and 11 p.m. With each unloading operation taking on average 10 minutes, this means that each site would be active for 40 minutes out of each hour during the morning rush hour. In early 2009 the scheme was suspended owing to the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2008">financial crisis</a> impeding fund-raising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hearse_trams">Hearse trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hearse trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Specially appointed <a href="/wiki/Hearse" title="Hearse">hearse</a> trams, or funeral trolley cars, were used for <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funeral</a> processions in many cities in the late 19th and early 20th century, particularly cities with large tram systems. The earliest known example in North America was <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>, which was already operating 26 funeral cars in 1886.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-middleton-p93_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-middleton-p93-44">[44]</a></sup> In the United States, funeral cars were often given names. At the turn of the century, "almost every major city [in the US] had one or more"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-middleton-p93_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-middleton-p93-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:93</sup> such cars in operation.</p>
<p>In Milan, Italy, hearse trams were used from the 1880s (initially horse-drawn) to the 1920s. The main cemeteries, Cimitero Monumentale and Cimitero Maggiore, included funeral tram stations. Additional funeral stations were located at Piazza Firenze and at Porta Romana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In the mid-1940s at least one special hearse tram was used in Turin, Italy. It was introduced due to the wartime shortage of automotive fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newcastle,_NSW" title="Newcastle, NSW">Newcastle, NSW</a>, Australia also operated two hearse trams<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TramHearse_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-TramHearse-47">[47]</a></sup> between 1896 and 1948.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dog_car">Dog car</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Dog car">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> a "dog car" was used between 1937 and 1955 for transporting dogs and their owners to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Melbourne_Showgrounds" title="Royal Melbourne Showgrounds">Royal Melbourne Showgrounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contractors.27_mobile_offices">Contractors' mobile offices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Contractors' mobile offices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two former passenger cars from the <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> system were converted and used as mobile offices within the <a href="/wiki/Preston_Workshops" title="Preston Workshops">Preston Workshops</a> between 1969 and 1974, by personnel from <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Engineering" title="Commonwealth Engineering">Commonwealth Engineering</a> and <a href="/wiki/ASEA" title="ASEA">ASEA</a> who were connected with the construction of Melbourne's Z Class cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restaurant_trams">Restaurant trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Restaurant trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Tramcar_Restaurant_in_St_Kilda_-_24-11-2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Colonial_Tramcar_Restaurant_in_St_Kilda_-_24-11-2012.jpg/220px-Colonial_Tramcar_Restaurant_in_St_Kilda_-_24-11-2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Colonial_Tramcar_Restaurant_in_St_Kilda_-_24-11-2012.jpg/330px-Colonial_Tramcar_Restaurant_in_St_Kilda_-_24-11-2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Colonial_Tramcar_Restaurant_in_St_Kilda_-_24-11-2012.jpg/440px-Colonial_Tramcar_Restaurant_in_St_Kilda_-_24-11-2012.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Tramcar_Restaurant_in_St_Kilda_-_24-11-2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Tramcar_Restaurant" title="Colonial Tramcar Restaurant">Melbourne tramcar restaurant</a> in <a href="/wiki/St_Kilda,_Victoria" title="St Kilda, Victoria">St Kilda</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A number of systems have introduced restaurant trams, particularly as a tourist attraction. This is specifically a modern trend. Systems which have or have had restaurant trams include <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bendigo" title="Bendigo">Bendigo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, in Australia; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brussels_trams" title="Brussels trams">Brussels</a> in Belgium; <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a> in the Netherlands; <a href="/wiki/Christchurch" title="Christchurch">Christchurch</a> in New Zealand;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a> in Italy; <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, Russia; <a href="/wiki/Almaty" title="Almaty">Almaty</a>, Kazakhstan <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zurich" title="Zurich">Zurich</a>, Switzerland.</p>
<p>Restaurant trams are particularly popular in Melbourne where three of the iconic "W" class trams have been converted. All three often run in tandem and there are usually multiple meal sittings. Bookings often close months in advance.</p>
<p>Bistro trams with buffets operated between <a href="/wiki/Krefeld" title="Krefeld">Krefeld</a> and <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a> in Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a> in Finland has a <a href="/wiki/Sp%C3%A5rakoff" title="Spårakoff">pub tram</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>, Germany has a tourist circle line called "Ebbelwei-Express", in which the traditional local drink "Apfelwein" (locally called "Ebbelwei", a type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_cider" title="Hard cider">hard cider</a>) is served.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobile_Library_Service">Mobile Library Service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Mobile Library Service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Munich tram No.24, delivered in 1912, was refurbished as a mobile library in 1928. Known as "Städtische Wanderbücherei München", it was in public service until 1970. It was preserved and is now on public display in a railway museum in Hannover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edmonton" title="Edmonton">Edmonton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a> used a streetcar bookmobile from 1941 to 1956.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nursery_trams">Nursery trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Nursery trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_War_Two" title="World War Two">World War Two</a>, in both <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wroc%C5%82aw" title="Wrocław">Wrocław</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, so-called trams-nurseries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tramwaje_Warszawskie_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tramwaje_Warszawskie-54">[54]</a></sup> were in operation, collecting children from the workplaces of their parents (often tram employees). These mobile nurseries either carried the children around the system or delivered them to the nursery school run by transport company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazeta.pl_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gazeta.pl-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specialized_work_trams">Specialized work trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Specialized work trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most systems had cars that were converted to specific uses on the system, other than simply the carriage of passengers. As just one example, the <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> system used or uses the following "technical" cars : a Ballast Motor, Ballast Trailers, a Blow Car, Breakdown Cars, Conductors and/or Drivers' Instruction Cars, a Laboratory Testing Car, a Line Marking Car, a Pantograph Testing Car, Per Way Locomotives, Rail Grinders, a Rail Hardner Loco., a Scrapper Car, Scrubbers, Sleeper Carriers, Track Cleaners, a Welding Car, a Wheel Transport Car and a Workshops Locomotive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising">Advertising</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Advertising">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many systems have passenger carrying vehicles with all-over advertising on the exterior and/or the interior.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourist_Trams">Tourist Trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Tourist Trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many systems have retained historical trams which will often run over parts of the system for tourists and/or tram enthusiasts.</p>
<p>In Melbourne, Australia, a number of the iconic <a href="/wiki/W-class_Melbourne_tram" title="W-class Melbourne tram">W class</a> run throughout each day in a set route which circles the Central Business District. They are primarily for the use of tourists, although often also used by regular commuters.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tramway_operation">Tramway operation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Tramway operation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1972" data-file-width="4534" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_2.jpg/220px-%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_2.jpg/330px-%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_2.jpg/440px-%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B0_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Vasileostrovsky_tram_depot" title="Vasileostrovsky tram depot">Vasileostrovsky tram depot</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museum_of_electrical_transport_in_Saint_Petersburg" title="Museum of electrical transport in Saint Petersburg">Museum of electrical transport</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Petersburg" title="St. Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are two main types of tramways, the classic tramway built in the early 20th century with the tram system operating in mixed traffic, and the later type which is most often associated with the tram system having its own right of way. Tram systems that have their own right of way are often called <a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">light rail</a> but this does not always hold true. Though these two systems differ in their operation, their equipment is much the same.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crich-Bogie-wyrdlight-812322.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="733" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Crich-Bogie-wyrdlight-812322.jpg/170px-Crich-Bogie-wyrdlight-812322.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Crich-Bogie-wyrdlight-812322.jpg/255px-Crich-Bogie-wyrdlight-812322.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Crich-Bogie-wyrdlight-812322.jpg/340px-Crich-Bogie-wyrdlight-812322.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crich-Bogie-wyrdlight-812322.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Engineers working on an electro-mechanical bogie from a tram at Crich Tramway Village</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tram_stop">Tram stop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Tram stop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tram_stop" title="Tram stop">Tram stop</a></div>
<p>Tram stops may be similar to <a href="/wiki/Bus_stop" title="Bus stop">bus stops</a> in design and use, particularly in street-running sections, where in some cases other vehicles are legally required to stop clear of the tram doors. Some stops may resemble to <a href="/wiki/Railway_platform" title="Railway platform">railway platforms</a>, particularly in private right-of-way sections and where trams are boarded at standard <a href="/wiki/Railway_platform_height" title="Railway platform height">railway platform height</a>, as opposed to using steps at the doorway or <a href="/wiki/Low-floor_tram" title="Low-floor tram">low-floor trams</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controls">Controls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Controls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Trams were traditionally operated with separate levers for applying power and brakes. More modern vehicles use a <a href="/wiki/Locomotive" title="Locomotive">locomotive</a>-style controller which incorporate a <a href="/wiki/Dead_man%27s_switch" title="Dead man's switch">dead man's switch</a>. The success of the <a href="/wiki/PCC_streetcar" title="PCC streetcar">PCC streetcar</a> had also seen trams use automobile-style <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile_pedal" title="Automobile pedal">foot controls</a> allowing hands-free operation, particularly when the driver was responsible for fare collection.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Track">Track</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Track">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tramway_track" title="Tramway track">Tramway track</a></div>
<p>Tramway track can have different <a href="/wiki/Rail_profile" title="Rail profile">rail profiles</a> to accommodate the various operating environments of the vehicle. They may be embedded into <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> for street-running operation, or use standard <a href="/wiki/Ballast" title="Ballast">ballasted</a> track with <a href="/wiki/Railroad_tie" title="Railroad tie">railroad ties</a> on high-speed sections. A more ecological solution is to embed tracks into <a href="/wiki/Sod" title="Sod">grass turf</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_supply">Power supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Power supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Electric trams use various devices to collect power from <a href="/wiki/Overhead_line" title="Overhead line">overhead lines</a>. The most common device found today is the <a href="/wiki/Pantograph_(transport)" title="Pantograph (transport)">pantograph</a>, while some older systems use <a href="/wiki/Trolley_pole" title="Trolley pole">trolley poles</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bow_collector" title="Bow collector">bow collectors</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ground-level_power_supply" title="Ground-level power supply">Ground-level power supply</a> has become a recent innovation. Another new technology uses <a href="/wiki/Supercapacitor" title="Supercapacitor">supercapacitors</a>; when an <a href="/wiki/Insulator_(electricity)" title="Insulator (electricity)">insulator</a> at a track switch cuts off power from the tram for a short distance along the line, the tram can use energy stored in a large <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitor</a> to drive the tram past the gap in the power feed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> A rather obsolete system for power supply is <a href="/wiki/Conduit_current_collection" title="Conduit current collection">conduit current collection</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tram_and_light_rail_transit_systems_around_the_world">Tram and light rail transit systems around the world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Tram and light rail transit systems around the world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tram_and_light_rail_transit_systems" title="Tram and light rail transit systems">Tram and light rail transit systems</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_tram_and_light_rail_transit_systems" title="List of tram and light rail transit systems">List of tram and light rail transit systems</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Levsk%C3%A9ho,_Tatra_T3_p%C5%99i_vjezdu,_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2416" data-file-width="2708" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Levsk%C3%A9ho%2C_Tatra_T3_p%C5%99i_vjezdu%2C_detail.jpg/220px-Levsk%C3%A9ho%2C_Tatra_T3_p%C5%99i_vjezdu%2C_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Levsk%C3%A9ho%2C_Tatra_T3_p%C5%99i_vjezdu%2C_detail.jpg/330px-Levsk%C3%A9ho%2C_Tatra_T3_p%C5%99i_vjezdu%2C_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Levsk%C3%A9ho%2C_Tatra_T3_p%C5%99i_vjezdu%2C_detail.jpg/440px-Levsk%C3%A9ho%2C_Tatra_T3_p%C5%99i_vjezdu%2C_detail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Levsk%C3%A9ho,_Tatra_T3_p%C5%99i_vjezdu,_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech</a> <a href="/wiki/Tatra_T3" title="Tatra T3">Tatra T3</a> – 14,113 units sold worldwide make it one of the highest-selling types of tram.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout the world there are many tram systems; some dating from the late 19th or early 20th centuries. However a large number of the old systems were closed during the mid-20th century because of such perceived drawbacks as route inflexibility and maintenance expense. This was especially the case in North American, Australian, British, French and other West European cities. Some traditional tram systems did however survive and remain operating much as when first built over a century ago. In the past twenty years their numbers have been augmented by modern tramway or light rail systems in cities that had discarded this form of transport.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Tram and light rail systems operate in 388 cities across the world, 206 of which are in Europe;</li>
<li>Since 1985 120 light rail systems have opened;</li>
<li>Since 2000 78 systems have opened while 13 have closed. The countries that have opened the most systems since 2000 are the USA (23), France (20), Spain (16), and Turkey (8);</li>
<li>15,618 km of track is in operation, with 850 km in construction and a further 2,350 km planned;</li>
<li>The longest systems are in Melbourne (256 km), Saint Petersburg (205 km), Katowice (Upper Silesian Industrial Region) (200 km), Cologne (193 km), Berlin (192 km); Moscow (181 km) and Vienna and Milan (170 km).</li>
<li>These lines have 32,345 stops at an average spacing of 484 metres;</li>
<li>They carry 13.5 billion passengers a year, 3% of all public transport passengers. The highest-volume systems are Budapest (396 million passengers a year), Prague (333m), Bucharest (322m), Saint Petersburg (312m), and Vienna (305m);</li>
<li>The most intensely used networks (passengers per km of, per year) are: Istanbul, Hong Kong, Tokyo and Sarajevo.</li>
<li>Just over 36,000 trams and light rail vehicles are in operation. The largest fleets are in Prague (920), Moscow (919), Saint Petersburg (833), Budapest (612) and Warsaw (526);</li>
<li>Between 1997 and 2014, 400-450 vehicles have been built per year.</li>
</ul>
<p>Source, as of October 2015:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popularity">Popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Popularity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Tramways with tramcars</i> (<a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a>) or <i>street railways with streetcars</i> (<a href="/wiki/North_American_English" title="North American English">North American English</a>) were common throughout the industrialised world in the late 19th and early 20th centuries but they had disappeared from most British, Canadian, French and US cities by the mid-20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Europe" title="Trams in Europe">trams in parts of continental Europe</a> continued to be used by many cities, although there were contractions in some countries, including the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 1980 trams have returned to favour in many places, partly because their tendency to dominate the roadway, formerly seen as a disadvantage, is now considered to be a merit. New systems have been built in the United States, Great Britain, Ireland, France, Australia and many other countries.</p>
<p>In Milan, Italy, the old "<a href="/wiki/Peter_Witt_streetcar" title="Peter Witt streetcar">Ventotto</a>" trams are considered by its inhabitants a "symbol" of the city. The same can be said of trams in <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Melbourne" title="Trams in Melbourne">Melbourne</a> in general, but particularly the iconic <a href="/wiki/W-class_Melbourne_tram" title="W-class Melbourne tram">W class</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Toronto_streetcar_system" title="Toronto streetcar system">Toronto streetcar system</a> had similarly become an iconic symbol of the city, operating the largest network in the Americas as well as the only large-scale tram system in Canada (not including light rail systems, or heritage lines).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_tram_systems">Major tram systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Major tram systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>The largest tram ((classic tram, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streetcar" title="Streetcar">streetcar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stra%C3%9Fenbahn" title="Straßenbahn">straßenbahn</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_tram" title="Fast tram">fast tram</a>, (<a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">light rail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stadtbahn" title="Stadtbahn">stadtbahn</a>)) networks in the world by route length (as of 2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tundria.com_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-tundria.com-62">[62]</a></sup> are <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Melbourne" title="Trams in Melbourne">Melbourne</a> (256 km (159 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tramways_in_Saint_Petersburg" title="Tramways in Saint Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a> (205.5 km (127.7 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Stadtbahn" title="Cologne Stadtbahn">Cologne</a> (194.8 km (121.0 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin_tram" title="Berlin tram">Berlin</a> 191.6 km (119.1 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Moscow" title="Trams in Moscow">Moscow</a> (183 km (114 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Budapest" title="Trams in Budapest">Budapest</a> (172 km (107 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silesian_Interurbans" title="Silesian Interurbans">Katowice agglomeration</a> (171 km (106 mi))<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Vienna" title="Trams in Vienna">Vienna</a> (170 km (110 mi)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other large systems include (but are not limited to): <a href="/wiki/DART_Light_Rail" title="DART Light Rail">Dallas Light Rail</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a (96 mi)), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sofia_Tramway" title="Sofia Tramway">Sofia</a> (153.6 km (95.4 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tundria.com_62-1"><a (125.32 km (77.87 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart_Stadtbahn" title="Stuttgart Stadtbahn">Stuttgart</a> (124.5 km (77.4 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="/wiki/Munich_tramway" title="Munich tramway">Munich</a> (79 km (49 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Antwerp" title="Trams in Antwerp">Antwerp</a> (79 km (49 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denver_LRT" title="Denver LRT">Denver</a> (76 km (47 mi)), <a href="/wiki/RATP_Ia%C8%99i" title="RATP Iași">Iași</a> (76 km (47 mi)), <a href="/wiki/TRAX_(light_rail)" title="TRAX (light rail)">Salt Lake Valley light rail</a> and <a href="/wiki/S_Line_(Utah_Transit_Authority)" title="S Line (Utah Transit Authority)">streetcar</a> (75.42 km (46.86 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Dortmund_Stadtbahn" title="Dortmund Stadtbahn">Dortmund</a> (75 km (47 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Rotterdam" title="Trams in Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a> (75 km (47 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/MetroLink_(St._Louis)" title="MetroLink (St. Louis)">St. Louis Metropolitan Area</a> (74 km (46 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Lviv" title="Trams in Lviv">Lviv</a> (73.5 km (45.7 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Mykolaiv" title="Trams in Mykolaiv">Mykolaiv</a> (72.83 km (45.25 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Karlsruhe" title="Trams in Karlsruhe">Karlsruhe</a> (71.5 km (44.4 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Brno" title="Trams in Brno">Brno</a> (70.4 km (43.7 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Porto_Metro" title="Porto Metro">Porto</a> (70 km (43 mi)), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacramento_light_rail#Light_rail" title="Sacramento light rail">Sacramento</a> (69 km (43 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Frankfurt_am_Main" title="Trams in Frankfurt am Main">Frankfurt am Main</a> (68 km (42 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clara_Valley_Transportation_Authority_light_rail" title="Santa Clara Valley Transportation Authority light rail">San Jose and its suburbs</a> (67.9 km (42.2 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Lyon_tramway" title="Lyon tramway">Lyon</a> (66.3 km (41.2 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Trams_in_Ostrava&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Trams in Ostrava (page does not exist)">Ostrava</a> (65.7 km (40.8 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Basel" title="Trams in Basel">Basel</a> (65.7 km (40.8 mi), <a href="/wiki/Donetsk#Transport" title="Donetsk">Donetsk</a> (65.7 km (40.8 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Pozna%C5%84" title="Trams in Poznań">Poznań</a> (65.6 km (40.8 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Minsk" title="Trams in Minsk">Minsk</a> (62.8 km (39.0 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Szczecin" title="Trams in Szczecin">Szczecin</a> (60 km (37 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Graz" title="Trams in Graz">Graz</a> (59.8 km (37.2 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Montpellier_tramway" title="Montpellier tramway">Montpellier</a> (55.6 km (34.5 mi)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Pyongyang" title="Trams in Pyongyang">Pyongyang</a> (53.5 km (33.2 mi)), <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Essen" title="Trams in Essen">Essen</a> (52.4 km (32.6 mi)) and <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Gda%C5%84sk" title="Trams in Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a> (52.2 km (32.4 mi)). This list is not exhaustive.</p>
<p>The length of the following networks is disputed: Philadelphia trolleycar network comprise from <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Subway%E2%80%93Surface_Trolley_Lines" title="SEPTA Subway–Surface Trolley Lines">Subway–Surface Trolley Lines</a> with <a href="/wiki/Network_length_(transport)#Route_length" title="Network length (transport)">line length</a> 31.9 km (19.8 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publictransit.us_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-publictransit.us-95">[95]</a></sup> or by another source 100.8 km (62.6 mi) (the sum of all lines (lines <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Route_10" title="SEPTA Route 10">10</a> (18.7 km (11.6 mi)), <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Route_11" title="SEPTA Route 11">11</a> (21.4 km (13.3 mi)), <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Route_13" title="SEPTA Route 13">13</a> (18.3 km (11.4 mi)), <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Route_34" title="SEPTA Route 34">34</a> (16.3 km (10.1 mi)) and <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Route_36" title="SEPTA Route 36">36</a> (26.1 km (16.2 mi)))<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Routes_101_and_102" title="SEPTA Routes 101 and 102">light rail routes 101 and 102</a> with length 19.2 km (11.9 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publictransit.us_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-publictransit.us-95">[95]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Route_15" title="SEPTA Route 15">heritage route 15</a> (line length 13.7 km (8.5 mi)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publictransit.us_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-publictransit.us-95">[95]</a></sup> Put together, it measures 64.6 km (40.1 mi) or 133.5 km (83.0 mi) of line length. The <a href="/wiki/Network_length_(transport)#Route_length" title="Network length (transport)">route length</a> is 86.6 km (53.8 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publictransit.us_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-publictransit.us-95">[95]</a></sup> Next, <a href="/wiki/Muni_Metro" title="Muni Metro">San Francisco light rail</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_the_San_Francisco_Bay_Area#Light_rail" title="Transportation in the San Francisco Bay Area">streetcars</a> have route length 50 km (31 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-96">[96]</a></sup> or 59.4 km (36.9 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Except it, in city is 8.3 km (5.2 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-96">[96]</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_cable_car_system" title="San Francisco cable car system">cable car</a>. Next networks with disputed route length are <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Milan" title="Trams in Milan">Milan</a> (126.5 km (78.6 mi))<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> or 181 km (112 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf_Stadtbahn" title="Düsseldorf Stadtbahn">Düsseldorf Stadtbahn</a> (76 km (47 mi))<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-100">[100]</a></sup> or 78 km (48 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rheinbahn.de_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-rheinbahn.de-101">[101]</a></sup>/<a href="/wiki/Trams_in_D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Trams in Düsseldorf">streetcars</a> 72 km (45 mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-100">[100]</a></sup> or 68.5 km (42.6 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rheinbahn.de_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-rheinbahn.de-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_The_Hague" title="Trams in The Hague">The Hague</a> (105 km (65 mi))<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> or by other sources 142 km (88 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg_tramway" title="Strasbourg tramway">Strasbourg</a> (40.4 km (25.1 mi))<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> or 57.5 km (35.7 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Kolkata" title="Trams in Kolkata">Kolkata</a> (57 km (35 mi))<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> or 68 km (42 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Nizhny_Novgorod_(formerly_Gorky)" title="Trams in Nizhny Novgorod (formerly Gorky)">Nizhny Novgorod (formerly Gorky)</a> (98 km (61 mi))<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> or 76.5 km (47.5 mi)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. This list is not exhaustive.</p>
<p>The longest single tram line in the world is the 68 km (42 mi) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belgian_Coast_Tram" title="Belgian Coast Tram">Belgian Coast Tram</a>, which runs almost the entire length of the Belgian coast. Another fairly long line is the <a href="/wiki/Valley_Metro_Rail" title="Valley Metro Rail">Valley Metro Rail</a> in <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix, Arizona</a>, with its 42 km (26 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, the <a href="/wiki/Tramways_in_%C3%8Ele-de-France" title="Tramways in Île-de-France">Paris Tram System</a> was, at its peak, the world's largest system, with 1,111 km (690 mi) of track in 1925 (according to other sources, ca. 640 km of route length in 1930). However it was completely closed in 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The next largest system appears to have been 857 km (533 mi), in <a href="/wiki/Tram#South_America" title="Tram">Buenos Aires</a> before 19 February 1963. The third largest was Chicago, with over 850 km (530 mi) of track,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> but it was all converted to trolleybus and bus services by 21 June 1958. Before its decline, the <a href="/wiki/Berliner_Verkehrsbetriebe" title="Berliner Verkehrsbetriebe">BVG</a> in Berlin operated a very large network with 634 km (394 mi) of route. Before its system started to be converted to trolleybus (and later bus) services in the 1930s (last tramway closed 6 July 1952), the first-generation London network had 555 km (345 mi) of route in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> In 1958 trams in Rio de Jainero were employed on (433 km (269 mi)) of track. The final line, the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Teresa_Tram" title="Santa Teresa Tram">Santa teresa route</a> was closed in 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> During a period in the 1980s, the world's largest tram system was in <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Saint_Petersburg" title="Trams in Saint Petersburg">Leningrad</a> (now known as St. Petersburg) with 350 km (220 mi), USSR, and was included as such in the <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a>; however Saint Petersburg's tram system has declined in size since the fall of the Soviet Union. Wiena (Vienna) in 1960 had 340 km (211 mi), before the expansion of bus services and the opening of a subway (1976). Substituting subway services for tram routes continues. 320 km (199 mi) was in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minneapolis-Saint_Paul" title="Minneapolis-Saint Paul">Minneapolis-Saint Paul</a> in 1947: There streetcars ended 31 October 1953 in Minneapolis and 19 June 1954 in St. Paul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Sydney" title="Trams in Sydney">Sydney tram network</a>, before it was closed on 25 February 1961, had 291 km (181 mi) of route, and was thus the largest in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>. As from 1961, the Melbourne system (currently recognised as the world's largest) took over Sydney's title as the largest network in Australia.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_town_tramway_systems_in_Africa" title="List of town tramway systems in Africa">List of town tramway systems in Africa</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Asia" title="Trams in Asia">Trams in Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dalian_Liaoning_China_Historical-Tramway-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Dalian_Liaoning_China_Historical-Tramway-01.jpg/220px-Dalian_Liaoning_China_Historical-Tramway-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Dalian_Liaoning_China_Historical-Tramway-01.jpg/330px-Dalian_Liaoning_China_Historical-Tramway-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Dalian_Liaoning_China_Historical-Tramway-01.jpg/440px-Dalian_Liaoning_China_Historical-Tramway-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dalian_Liaoning_China_Historical-Tramway-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A historical tramway in <a href="/wiki/Dalian" title="Dalian">Dalian</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HKtram.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/HKtram.JPG/220px-HKtram.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/HKtram.JPG/330px-HKtram.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/HKtram.JPG/440px-HKtram.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HKtram.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A double-decker <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Tramways" title="Hong Kong Tramways">tram in Hong Kong</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tramway systems were well established in the Asian region at the start of the 20th century, but started a steady decline during the mid to late 1930s. The 1960s marked the end of its dominance in public transportation with most major systems closed and the equipment and rails sold for scrap; however, some extensive original lines still remain in service in Hong Kong and Japan. In recent years there has been renewed interest in the tram with modern systems being built in Japan, the Philippines, and South Korea.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kolkata_transport.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="552" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Kolkata_transport.jpg/220px-Kolkata_transport.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Kolkata_transport.jpg/330px-Kolkata_transport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Kolkata_transport.jpg/440px-Kolkata_transport.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kolkata_transport.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kolkata_tram" title="Kolkata tram">tram in Kolkata</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> trams still operate in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcutta" title="Calcutta">Calcutta</a>. Trams were discontinued in <a href="/wiki/Chennai" title="Chennai">Chennai</a> in 1954 and in <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a> in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Northern and Central areas of the City of <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> had an electric Tram Car system (42" Gauge). This system commenced operations about 1900 and was discontinued by 1960 and Tram system is in the process of being bought to <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a> as part of the plan of <a href="/wiki/Western_Region_Megapolis" title="Western Region Megapolis">Western Region Megapolis</a>. Other countries with discontinued tram systems include Indonesia, Malaysia, Thailand, Pakistan and Vietnam. However, a tram system is planned for construction in <a href="/wiki/Gwadar" title="Gwadar">Gwadar</a>, Pakistan where construction started in late 2011. Trams are also under construction in <a href="/wiki/DHA_City,_Karachi" title="DHA City, Karachi">DHA City, Karachi</a>. In China the cities of Beijing, Zhuhai, Nanjing and Shenzhen are planning tram networks for the future.</p>
<p>The first Japanese tram line was inaugurated in 1895 as the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kyoto_Electric_Railroad&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kyoto Electric Railroad (page does not exist)">Kyoto Electric Railroad</a>. The tram reached its zenith in 1932 when 82 rail companies operated 1,479 kilometers of track in 65 cities. The tram declined in popularity through the remaining years of the 1930s and during the 1960s many of the remaining operational tramways were shut down and dismantled.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Europe" title="Trams in Europe">Trams in Europe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bombardier_Flexity_Berlin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1431" data-file-width="2500" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Bombardier_Flexity_Berlin.jpg/220px-Bombardier_Flexity_Berlin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Bombardier_Flexity_Berlin.jpg/330px-Bombardier_Flexity_Berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Bombardier_Flexity_Berlin.jpg/440px-Bombardier_Flexity_Berlin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bombardier_Flexity_Berlin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
New Berlin MetroTrams</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In many European cities much tramway infrastructure was lost in the mid-20th century, though not always on the same scale as in other parts of the world such as North America. Most of Central and Eastern Europe retained the majority of its tramway systems and it is here that the largest and busiest tram systems in the world are found. Many European cities are rehabilitating, upgrading, expanding and reconstructing their old tramway lines and building new tramway lines. Whereas most systems and vehicles in the tram sector are found in Central and Eastern Europe, in the 1960s and 1970s, tram systems were shut down in many places in Western Europe, however urban transportation has been experiencing a sustained long running revival since the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%B9_Gotha_T57_(%E2%84%96_020).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/%D0%A2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%B9_Gotha_T57_%28%E2%84%96_020%29.JPG/220px-%D0%A2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%B9_Gotha_T57_%28%E2%84%96_020%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/%D0%A2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%B9_Gotha_T57_%28%E2%84%96_020%29.JPG/330px-%D0%A2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%B9_Gotha_T57_%28%E2%84%96_020%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/%D0%A2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%B9_Gotha_T57_%28%E2%84%96_020%29.JPG/440px-%D0%A2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%B9_Gotha_T57_%28%E2%84%96_020%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%B9_Gotha_T57_(%E2%84%96_020).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tram in Molochne (Ukraine) - the smallest tram system in the world</div>
