<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Streetcars_in_North_America" title="Streetcars in North America">Streetcars in North America</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SF_Streetcar_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2912" data-file-width="4368" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/SF_Streetcar_3.jpg/220px-SF_Streetcar_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/SF_Streetcar_3.jpg/330px-SF_Streetcar_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/SF_Streetcar_3.jpg/440px-SF_Streetcar_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SF_Streetcar_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
San Francisco <a href="/wiki/PCC_streetcar" title="PCC streetcar">PCC</a> <a href="/wiki/Heritage_streetcar" title="Heritage streetcar">heritage streetcar</a> on <a href="/wiki/F_Market_%26_Wharves" title="F Market &amp; Wharves">Market Street</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In North America, these vehicles are called "streetcars" (or "trolleys"); the term <i>tram</i> is more likely to be understood as a <a href="/wiki/Tourist_trolley" title="Tourist trolley">tourist trolley</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Aerial_tramway" title="Aerial tramway">aerial tramway</a>, or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People-mover" title="People-mover">people-mover</a>.</p>
<p>In most North American cities, streetcar lines were largely torn up in the mid-20th century for a variety of financial, technological and social reasons. Exceptions included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MBTA_Green_Line" title="MBTA Green Line">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> (with a much shrunken network), <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>. Pittsburgh had kept most of its streetcar system serving the city and many suburbs until severe cutbacks on 27 January 1967, making it the longest-lasting large-network US streetcar system,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> though Pittsburgh's surviving streetcar lines were converted to light rail in the 1980s.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CLRVs_4049_and_4090_Eastbound_on_King.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/CLRVs_4049_and_4090_Eastbound_on_King.jpg/220px-CLRVs_4049_and_4090_Eastbound_on_King.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/CLRVs_4049_and_4090_Eastbound_on_King.jpg/330px-CLRVs_4049_and_4090_Eastbound_on_King.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/CLRVs_4049_and_4090_Eastbound_on_King.jpg/440px-CLRVs_4049_and_4090_Eastbound_on_King.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CLRVs_4049_and_4090_Eastbound_on_King.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Toronto_streetcar_system" title="Toronto streetcar system">Toronto streetcar system</a> is the largest in North America.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> currently has the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_streetcar_system" title="Toronto streetcar system">largest streetcar system</a> in the Americas in terms of track length and ridership, operated by the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Transit_Commission" title="Toronto Transit Commission">Toronto Transit Commission</a>. This is the only large-scale streetcar system existing in Canada, not including the light rail systems that some Canadian cities currently operate, or <a href="/wiki/Heritage_streetcar" title="Heritage streetcar">heritage streetcar</a> lines operating only seasonally. Toronto's system currently uses <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Light_Rail_Vehicle" title="Canadian Light Rail Vehicle">Canadian Light Rail Vehicles</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Articulated_Light_Rail_Vehicle" title="Articulated Light Rail Vehicle">Articulated Light Rail Vehicles</a>, after a history of using <a href="/wiki/PCC_streetcar" title="PCC streetcar">PCCs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Witt_streetcar" title="Peter Witt streetcar">Peter Witt cars</a>, and horse-drawn carriages. The TTC has begun accepting delivery of a fleet of 204 of a variant of <a href="/wiki/Bombardier_Inc." title="Bombardier Inc.">Bombardier</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flexity_Outlook_(Toronto_LRV)" title="Flexity Outlook (Toronto LRV)">Flexity Outlook</a> (also used in some European tram systems) as a replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TorStar2014-09-08_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-TorStar2014-09-08-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TorStarHume2014-09-01_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-TorStarHume2014-09-01-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TorStar2014-08-31_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-TorStar2014-08-31-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Newer light rail lines in Toronto and Kitchener-Waterloo will be using the <a href="/wiki/Flexity_Freedom" title="Flexity Freedom">Flexity Freedom</a>.</p>
<p>Streetcars once existed in <a href="/wiki/Edmonton_Light_Rail_Transit" title="Edmonton Light Rail Transit">Edmonton</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calgary_Light_Rail" title="Calgary Light Rail">Calgary</a>, but both Canadian cities shut down their streetcar systems. In the late 1970s and early 1980s, both cities built and expanded new light rail systems. Streetcars also once operated in cities such as <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quebec_City" title="Quebec City">Quebec City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kitchener,_Ontario" title="Kitchener, Ontario">Kitchener</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Ontario" title="Hamilton, Ontario">Hamilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingston,_Ontario" title="Kingston, Ontario">Kingston</a>, <a href="/wiki/London,_Ontario" title="London, Ontario">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario_Streetcar_System" title="Windsor, Ontario Streetcar System">Windsor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peterborough,_Ontario" title="Peterborough, Ontario">Peterborough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regina,_Saskatchewan" title="Regina, Saskatchewan">Regina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saskatoon" title="Saskatoon">Saskatoon</a>. Some of these cities have restored their old streetcars and now run them as a heritage feature for tourists, such as the <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Downtown_Historic_Railway" title="Vancouver Downtown Historic Railway">Vancouver Downtown Historic Railway</a>.</p>
<p>San Francisco's <a href="/wiki/Muni_Metro" title="Muni Metro">Muni Metro</a> system is the largest surviving streetcar system in the United States, and has even revived previously closed streetcar lines such as the <a href="/wiki/F_Market_%26_Wharves" title="F Market &amp; Wharves">F Market &amp; Wharves</a> heritage streetcar line.</p>
<p>In a trend started in the 1980s, some American cities have brought back streetcars, examples of these being <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Oregon" title="Portland, Oregon">Portland</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tampa" title="Tampa">Tampa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Little_Rock" title="Little Rock">Little Rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Streetcar" title="Seattle Streetcar">Seattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a>. Prior to 2000, most of these new-generation streetcar systems were <a href="/wiki/Heritage_streetcar" title="Heritage streetcar">heritage streetcar</a> lines, using vintage or replica-vintage vehicles, but following the 2001 opening of the <a href="/wiki/Portland_Streetcar" title="Portland Streetcar">Portland Streetcar</a> system – the first to use modern vehicles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-T.26UT-01_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-T.26UT-01-121">[121]</a></sup> – most new US systems have been designed to use modern, <a href="/wiki/Low-floor_tram" title="Low-floor tram">low-floor</a> cars. Several additional cities are planning or proposing new streetcar systems, and such systems are under construction in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charlotte" title="Charlotte">Charlotte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">Cincinnati</a>, Dallas (a <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Area_Rapid_Transit#Downtown_Dallas_Streetcar_Project" title="Dallas Area Rapid Transit">second system</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kansas_City" title="Kansas City">Kansas City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">Milwaukee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City_Streetcar" title="Oklahoma City Streetcar">Oklahoma City</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tucson" title="Tucson">Tucson</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_DC" title="Washington DC">Washington DC</a>. Alternatively, in the late 20th century, several cities installed modern <a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">light rail</a> systems, in part along the same corridors as their old streetcars systems, the first of these being the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Trolley" title="San Diego Trolley">San Diego Trolley</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a> in 1981.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Australia" title="Trams in Australia">Trams in Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_New_Zealand" title="Trams in New Zealand">Trams in New Zealand</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CT_202_glenelg,_2012.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/CT_202_glenelg%2C_2012.JPG/220px-CT_202_glenelg%2C_2012.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/CT_202_glenelg%2C_2012.JPG/330px-CT_202_glenelg%2C_2012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/CT_202_glenelg%2C_2012.JPG/440px-CT_202_glenelg%2C_2012.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CT_202_glenelg,_2012.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citadis" title="Citadis">Citadis 302</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Historically, there have been trams in the following Australian cities and towns: <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>; <a href="/wiki/Rockhampton" title="Rockhampton">Rockhampton</a>, Queensland; <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newcastle,_Australia" title="Newcastle, Australia">Newcastle</a>, NSW; <a href="/wiki/Maitland" title="Maitland">Maitland</a>, NSW; <a href="/wiki/Broken_Hill" title="Broken Hill">Broken Hill</a>, NSW; <a href="/wiki/Yass,_New_South_Wales" title="Yass, New South Wales">Yass</a>, NSW; <a href="/wiki/Camden,_New_South_Wales" title="Camden, New South Wales">Camden</a>, NSW; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melbourne,_Australia" title="Melbourne, Australia">Melbourne</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria,_Australia" title="Victoria, Australia">Victoria</a>; <a href="/wiki/Geelong" title="Geelong">Geelong</a>, Victoria; <a href="/wiki/Ballarat" title="Ballarat">Ballarat</a>, Victoria; <a href="/wiki/Bendigo" title="Bendigo">Bendigo</a>, Victoria; <a href="/wiki/Sorrento,_Victoria" title="Sorrento, Victoria">Sorrento, Victoria</a>; <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Gawler,_South_Australia" title="Gawler, South Australia">Gawler, South Australia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Victor_Harbor_Horse_Drawn_Tram" title="Victor Harbor Horse Drawn Tram">Victor Harbor</a>, South Australia; <a href="/wiki/Moonta,_South_Australia" title="Moonta, South Australia">Moonta</a>-<a href="/wiki/Wallaroo,_South_Australia" title="Wallaroo, South Australia">Wallaroo, South Australia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Fremantle" title="Fremantle">Fremantle</a>, WA; <a href="/wiki/Kalgoorlie" title="Kalgoorlie">Kalgoorlie</a>, WA; <a href="/wiki/Laverton,_Western_Australia" title="Laverton, Western Australia">Laverton</a>, WA; <a href="/wiki/Hobart" title="Hobart">Hobart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Launceston,_Tasmania" title="Launceston, Tasmania">Launceston</a>, Tasmania. These ranged from extensive systems to single lines. The Sydney system, which closed in 1961, was the most extensive and the largest passenger carrier of any Australian public transport system then or since, moving over 400 million passengers per annum, at its peak.</p>
<p>Virtually all known types of motive power have been utilised at some stage, in Australia.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FC_2_test,_surfers_paradise_boulevard,_March_2014.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3090" data-file-width="4665" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/FC_2_test%2C_surfers_paradise_boulevard%2C_March_2014.JPG/220px-FC_2_test%2C_surfers_paradise_boulevard%2C_March_2014.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/FC_2_test%2C_surfers_paradise_boulevard%2C_March_2014.JPG/330px-FC_2_test%2C_surfers_paradise_boulevard%2C_March_2014.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/FC_2_test%2C_surfers_paradise_boulevard%2C_March_2014.JPG/440px-FC_2_test%2C_surfers_paradise_boulevard%2C_March_2014.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FC_2_test,_surfers_paradise_boulevard,_March_2014.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The new <a href="/wiki/Flexity_2" title="Flexity 2">Flexity 2</a> G:link tram on the Gold Coast</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Today, trams can be found in <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> (by length, the world's largest system), and to a lesser extent, <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>; all other major cities having largely dismantled their networks by the 1970s. <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Sydney" title="Trams in Sydney">Sydney reintroduced its tram</a> in 1997 on a modern <a href="/wiki/Light_rail_in_Sydney" title="Light rail in Sydney">light rail network</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Ballarat" title="Trams in Ballarat">Ballarat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Bendigo" title="Trams in Bendigo">Bendigo</a> retained their trams as heritage systems. In 2008 and 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Bendigo" title="Trams in Bendigo">Bendigo Tramway Co. Ltd.</a> conducted trials utilising their heritage trams for regular public transport. <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Victoria" title="Portland, Victoria">Portland, Victoria</a> introduced a tourist tram in 1996 - this uses a former Melbourne cable car dummy and trailer car, but utilising a hidden diesel motor. A completely new publlic transport system opened on the <a href="/wiki/Gold_Coast,_Queensland" title="Gold Coast, Queensland">Gold Coast, Queensland</a> on 20 July 2014. The new system is known as the <a href="/wiki/G:link" title="G:link">G:link</a> and is the first tram/ light rail system in Queensland, Australia since <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a> closed their tram network in 1969. As from March 2014, the Sydney line was extended to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dulwich_Hill" title="Dulwich Hill">Dulwich Hill</a>, with a further major line planned from <a href="/wiki/Circular_Quay" title="Circular Quay">Circular Quay</a> to the Sydney south-eastern suburbs. There are also plans for the reintroduction of trams in <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hobart" title="Hobart">Hobart</a> and the western suburbs of Sydney around <a href="/wiki/Parramatta" title="Parramatta">Parramatta</a>, and for completely new systems in <a href="/wiki/Canberra" title="Canberra">Canberra</a>, and on the <a href="/wiki/Sunshine_Coast,_Queensland" title="Sunshine Coast, Queensland">Sunshine Coast, Queensland</a>. (Ironically, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Burley_Griffin" title="Walter Burley Griffin">Walter Burley Griffin</a>'s 1912 prize-winning design for Canberra envisioned an extensive tram system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>) The construction of light rail in Canberra, to be known as <a href="/wiki/Capital_Metro,_Canberra" title="Capital Metro, Canberra">Capital Metro</a>, became the major issue of the October 2016 <a href="/wiki/Australian_Capital_Territory" title="Australian Capital Territory">ACT</a> election, with the ruling <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">ALP</a> strongly supporting Capital Metro, and the coalition of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Australia" title="Liberal Party of Australia">Liberal Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_of_Australia" title="National Party of Australia">National Party</a> promising not to proceed with the building of light rail but, instead, increase the number of buses. The ALP won the election <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> and it will proceed with its plans for the construction of Stage 1 of the light rail, which is expected to commence shortly.</p>
<p>The railway into the centre of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newcastle_railway_line" title="Newcastle railway line">Newcastle</a> was truncated to <a href="/wiki/Wickham_railway_station" title="Wickham railway station">Wickham</a> on 25 December 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The railway line was to be replaced with a tram but the implementation of this has been postponed a number of times! A bus replacement currently runs from Wickham to Newcastle.</p>
<p>A distinctive feature of many Australian trams was the early use of a lowered central section between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bogies" title="Bogies">bogies</a> (wheel-sets). This was intended to make passenger access easier, by reducing the number of steps required to reach the inside of the vehicle. It is believed that the design first originated in Christchurch, New Zealand, in the first decade of the 20th century. Cars with this design feature were frequently referred to as "drop-centres". Trams for Christchurch and Wellington built in the 1920s with an enclosed section at each end and an open-sided middle section were also known as <i>boon cars</i>, but did not have the drop-centre. Trams built since the 1970s have had conventional high or low floors.</p>
<p>New Zealand's last public transport tramway system, that of <a href="/wiki/Wellington" title="Wellington">Wellington</a>, closed in 1966. Nevertheless, there had been tramways ranging from large, comprehensive systems to single lines, in <a href="/wiki/Auckland" title="Auckland">Auckland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christchurch" title="Christchurch">Christchurch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dunedin" title="Dunedin">Dunedin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gisborne,_New_Zealand" title="Gisborne, New Zealand">Gisborne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invercargill" title="Invercargill">Invercargill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Napier,_New_Zealand" title="Napier, New Zealand">Napier</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Plymouth" title="New Plymouth">New Plymouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greymouth" title="Greymouth">Greymouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Westport,_New_Zealand" title="Westport, New Zealand">Westport</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokitika" title="Hokitika">Hokitika</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ross" title="Ross">Ross</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brighton,_New_Zealand" title="Brighton, New Zealand">Brighton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_New_Zealand" title="Charleston, New Zealand">Charleston</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kamiere&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kamiere (page does not exist)">Kamiere</a> and Kamara. An unusual feature of New Zealand's trams was the diversity of gauges. The 15 systems utilised no less than five gauges, thus making swapping of rolling stock from system to system, a bit of a challenge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Christchurch has subsequently reintroduced heritage trams over a new CBD route, but the overhead wiring plus some track was damaged by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christchurch_earthquake" title="Christchurch earthquake">earthquake</a> of 2011. In November 2013 a limited circuit was reopened. <a href="/wiki/Auckland" title="Auckland">Auckland</a> has recently introduced heritage trams into the Wynyard area, near the CBD, using former Melbourne trams. Preserved Auckland trams from the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Transport_and_Technology" title="Museum of Transport and Technology">MOTAT</a> have made cameo appearances during Heritage Weeks. Heritage lines exist at Auckland's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOTAT" title="MOTAT">MOTAT</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Wellington_Tramway_Museum" title="Wellington Tramway Museum">Wellington Tramway Museum</a> at <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_Park,_New_Zealand" title="Queen Elizabeth Park, New Zealand">Queen Elizabeth Park</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Kapiti_Coast" title="Kapiti Coast">Kapiti Coast</a>, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tramways_Trust_Wanganui&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tramways Trust Wanganui (page does not exist)">Tramways Trust Wanganui</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tramway_Historical_Society" title="Tramway Historical Society">Tramway Historical Society</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ferrymead" title="Ferrymead">Ferrymead</a> in Christchurch, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Christchurch_tramway_system" title="Christchurch tramway system">Christchurch Tramway Limited</a> in the central city.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tranv%C3%ADa_del_Este_(Buenos_Aires,_septiembre_2008).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Tranv%C3%ADa_del_Este_%28Buenos_Aires%2C_septiembre_2008%29.jpg/220px-Tranv%C3%ADa_del_Este_%28Buenos_Aires%2C_septiembre_2008%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Tranv%C3%ADa_del_Este_%28Buenos_Aires%2C_septiembre_2008%29.jpg/330px-Tranv%C3%ADa_del_Este_%28Buenos_Aires%2C_septiembre_2008%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Tranv%C3%ADa_del_Este_%28Buenos_Aires%2C_septiembre_2008%29.jpg/440px-Tranv%C3%ADa_del_Este_%28Buenos_Aires%2C_septiembre_2008%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tranv%C3%ADa_del_Este_(Buenos_Aires,_septiembre_2008).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Tranv%C3%ADa_del_Este" title="Tranvía del Este">Puerto Madero Tramway</a> in Buenos Aires</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a> in Argentina had once one of the most extensive tramway networks in the world with over 857 km (535 mi) of track, most of it dismantled during the 1960s in favor of bus transportation. A new line, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PreMetro_E2_(Buenos_Aires)" title="PreMetro E2 (Buenos Aires)">PreMetro line E2</a> system feeding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Line_E_(Buenos_Aires)" title="Line E (Buenos Aires)">Line E</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires_Subway" title="Buenos Aires Subway">Buenos Aires Subway</a> has been operating for the past few years on the outskirts of Buenos Aires.</p>
<p>Also in the city <a href="/wiki/Mendoza,_Argentina" title="Mendoza, Argentina">Mendoza</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, a new tramway system is in construction, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metrotranv%C3%ADa_of_Mendoza" title="Metrotranvía of Mendoza">Metrotranvía of Mendoza</a>, which will have a route of 12.5 km and will link five districts of the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Mendoza" title="Greater Mendoza">Greater Mendoza</a> conurbation. The opening of the system is scheduled for August 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Medell%C3%ADn" title="Medellín">Medellín</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, a tram line began operation on 15 October 2015,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> as a revival of old <a href="/wiki/Ayacucho_Tram" title="Ayacucho Tram">Ayacucho tram</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages">Advantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Advantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Trams (and road public transport in general) can be much more efficient in terms of road usage - one vehicle replacing about 40 cars which take up a far larger area of road space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></li>
<li>Vehicles run more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency_in_transportation" title="Fuel efficiency in transportation">efficiently</a> and overall operating costs are lower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></li>
<li>Tram vehicles are very durable, with some being in continuous revenue service for more than fifty years. This is especially compared to internal combustion buses, which tend to require high amounts of maintenance and break down after less than 20 years, mostly due to the vibrations of the engine.</li>
<li>In many cases tram networks have a higher capacity than similar buses. This has been cited as a reason for the replacement of one of Europe's busiest bus lines (with three-minute headways in peak times) with a tram by <a href="/wiki/Dresdner_Verkehrsbetriebe" title="Dresdner Verkehrsbetriebe">Dresdner Verkehrsbetriebe</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disadvantages">Disadvantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Disadvantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NET-tram_tracks_warning.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/NET-tram_tracks_warning.jpg/170px-NET-tram_tracks_warning.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/NET-tram_tracks_warning.jpg/255px-NET-tram_tracks_warning.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/NET-tram_tracks_warning.jpg/340px-NET-tram_tracks_warning.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NET-tram_tracks_warning.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sign warning cyclists of tram tracks</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Tram tracks can be hazardous for cyclists, as bikes, particularly those with narrow tyres, may get their wheels caught in the track grooves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bv.com.au_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-bv.com.au-134">[134]</a></sup> It is possible to close the grooves of the tracks on critical sections by rubber profiles that are pressed down by the wheelflanges of the passing tram but that cannot be lowered by the weight of a cyclist. If not well-maintained, however, these lose their effectiveness over time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>When wet, tram tracks tend to become slippery and thus dangerous for bicycles and motorcycles, especially in traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bv.com.au_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-bv.com.au-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> In some cases, even cars can be affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></li>
<li>The opening of new tram and light rail systems has sometimes been accompanied by a marked increase in car accidents, as a result of drivers' unfamiliarity with the physics and <a href="/wiki/Track_geometry" title="Track geometry">geometry</a> of trams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Though such increases may be temporary, long-term conflicts between motorists and light rail operations can be alleviated by segregating their respective rights-of-way and installing appropriate signage and warning systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li>Rail transport can expose neighbouring populations to moderate levels of low-frequency noise. However, transportation planners use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noise_mitigation" title="Noise mitigation">noise mitigation</a> strategies to minimize these effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Most of all, the potential for decreased private motor vehicle operations along the trolley's service line because of the service provision could result in lower <a href="/wiki/Ambient_noise_level" title="Ambient noise level">ambient noise levels</a> than without.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>There are many references to trams in popular culture, major references include:-</i></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_literature">In literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: In literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>One of the earliest literary references to trams occurs on the second page of <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">Henry James</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Europeans" title="The Europeans">The Europeans</a></i>:</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>"From time to time a strange vehicle drew near to the place where they stood—such a vehicle as the lady at the window, in spite of a considerable acquaintance with human inventions, had never seen before: a huge, low, omnibus, painted in brilliant colours, and decorated apparently with jingling bells, attached to a species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Groove_(machining)" title="Groove (machining)">groove</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pavement_(material)" title="Pavement (material)">pavement</a>, through which it was dragged, with a great deal of rumbling, bouncing, and scratching, by a couple of remarkably small horses." Published in 1878, the novel is set in the 1840s, though horse trams were not introduced in Boston till the 1850s. Note how the tram's efficiency surprises the European visitor; how two "remarkably small" horses sufficed to draw the "huge" tramcar.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Henry James also makes comical reference to the novelty and excitement of trams in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portrait_of_a_Lady" title="Portrait of a Lady">Portrait of a Lady</a> (1881)</i>:</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>"Henrietta Stackpole was struck with the fact that ancient Rome had been paved a good deal like New York, and even found an analogy between the deep chariot-ruts traceable in the antique street and the overjangled iron grooves which express the intensity of American life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Joseph Conrad described Amsterdam's trams in chapter 14 of <i>The Mirror of the Sea</i> (1906): "From afar at the end of Tsar Peter Straat, issued in the frosty air the tinkle of bells of the horse tramcars, appearing and disappearing in the opening between the buildings, like little toy carriages harnessed with toy horses and played with by people that appeared no bigger than children."</li>
<li>In episode 6 (<a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a>) of <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ulysses_(novel)" title="Ulysses (novel)">Ulysses</a></i> (1918), the party on the way to Paddy Dignam's funeral in a horse-drawn carriage idly debates the merits of various tramway improvements:</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>- I can't make out why the corporation doesn't run a tramline from the parkgate to the quays, Mr Bloom said. All those animals could be taken in trucks down to the boats.</dd>
<dd>- Instead of blocking up the thoroughfare, Martin Cunningham said. Quite so. They ought to.</dd>
<dd>- Yes, Mr Bloom said, and another thing I often thought is to have <a href="/wiki/Tram#Hearse_trams" title="Tram">municipal funeral trams</a> like they have in Milan, you know. Run the line out to the cemetery gates and have special trams, hearse and carriage and all. Don't you see what I mean?</dd>
<dd> – O that be damned for a story, Mr Dedalus said. Pullman car and saloon diningroom.</dd>
<dd> – A poor lookout for Corny [the undertaker], Mr Power added.</dd>
<dd> – Why? Mr Bloom asked, turning to Mr Dedalus. Wouldn't it be more decent than galloping two abreast?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>In his fictionalised but autobiographical <i><a href="/wiki/Memoirs_of_an_Infantry_Officer" title="Memoirs of an Infantry Officer">Memoirs of an Infantry Officer</a></i>, published in 1930, <a href="/wiki/Siegfried_Sassoon" title="Siegfried Sassoon">Siegfried Sassoon</a>'s narrator ruminates from his hospital bed in <a href="/wiki/Denmark_Hill" title="Denmark Hill">Denmark Hill</a>, London, in 1917 that "Even the screech and rumble of electric trams was a friendly sound; trams meant safety; the troops in the trenches thought about trams with affection."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danzig" title="Danzig">Danzig</a> trams figure extensively in the early stages of <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnter_Grass" title="Günter Grass">Günter Grass</a>'s <i>Die Blechtrommel</i> (<a href="/wiki/The_Tin_Drum" title="The Tin Drum">The Tin Drum</a>). In the last chapter the novel's hero <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oskar_Matzerath" title="Oskar Matzerath">Oskar Matzerath</a> and his friend Gottfried von Vittlar steal a tram late at night from outside Unterrath depot on the northern edge of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a>. In a <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">surreal</a> journey, von Vittlar drives the tram through the night, south to Flingern and Haniel and then east to the suburb of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf-Gerresheim" title="Düsseldorf-Gerresheim">Gerresheim</a>. Meanwhile, inside, Matzerath tries to rescue the half-blind Victor Weluhn (who had escaped from the <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">siege</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish_post_office_in_Danzig" title="Polish post office in Danzig">Polish post office in Danzig</a> at the beginning of the book and of the war) from his two green-hatted would-be <a href="/wiki/Executioner" title="Executioner">executioners</a>. Mazerath deposits his <a href="/wiki/Briefcase" title="Briefcase">briefcase</a>, which contains Sister Dorotea's severed <a href="/wiki/Ring_finger" title="Ring finger">ring finger</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Kilner_jar" title="Kilner jar">preserving jar</a>, on the dashboard "where professional motorman put their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunchbox" title="Lunchbox">lunchboxes</a>". They leave the tram at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminus_station" title="Terminus station">terminus</a> and the executioners tie Weluhn to a tree in von Vittlar's mother's garden and prepare to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machine-gun" title="Machine-gun">machine-gun</a> him. But Matzerath drums, Weluhn sings, and together they conjure up the Polish <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a>, who spirit both victim and executioners away. Matzerath asks von Vittlar to take his briefcase in the tram to the police HQ in the Fürstenwall, which he does. The latter part of this route is today served by tram route 703 terminating at Gerresheim <a href="/wiki/Stadtbahn" title="Stadtbahn">Stadtbahn</a> station ("by the glassworks" as Grass notes, referring to the famous glass factory).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></li>
<li>In his 1967 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spy" title="Spy">spy</a> <a href="/wiki/Thriller_(genre)" title="Thriller (genre)">thriller</a> <i><a href="/wiki/An_Expensive_Place_to_Die" title="An Expensive Place to Die">An Expensive Place to Die</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Len_Deighton" title="Len Deighton">Len Deighton</a> misidentifies the <a href="/wiki/Coast_Tram_(Belgium)" title="Coast Tram (Belgium)">Flemish coast tram</a>: "The red glow of <a href="/wiki/Ostend" title="Ostend">Ostend</a> is nearer now and yellow trains rattle alongside the motor road and over the bridge by the Royal Yacht Club..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i>Funeral in Berlin</i> the protagonist approaches Checkpoint Charlie driving "across the tram tracks of Zimmerstrasse that bump you into a world where 'communist' is not a dirty word".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W._V._Awdry" title="W. V. Awdry">Rev W. Awdry</a> wrote about <a href="/wiki/GER_Class_C53" title="GER Class C53">GER Class C53</a> called <a href="/wiki/Toby_the_Tram_Engine" title="Toby the Tram Engine">Toby the Tram Engine</a>, which starred in his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Railway_Series" title="The Railway Series">The Railway Series</a></i> with his faithful coach, Henrietta.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Chrome_Shelled_Regios" title="Chrome Shelled Regios">Chrome Shelled Regios</a>, a Japanese novel, trams are featured in the futuristic city of Zuelni.</li>
<li>In <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Almonds_and_Raisins&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Almonds and Raisins (page does not exist)">Almonds and Raisins</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Maisie_Mosco" title="Maisie Mosco">Maisie Mosco</a>, a novel about Jewish immigration to Manchester, England, from mainland Europe in the early twentieth century, the newly arrived Sanberg family see a tram for the first time - a <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Corporation_Tramways" title="Manchester Corporation Tramways">Manchester double-decker</a>. Abraham (the father) exclaims, "A train with two storeys? And no roof?" [Manchester trams were open-topped in those days.] The local rabbi explains: "In English, they call it a tram.... In Yiddish, we don't have a word for it." "In the wire overhead, there's electricity, we don't have a word for that either."</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_music">In music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: In music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/The_Trolley_Song" title="The Trolley Song">The Trolley Song</a>" in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Meet_Me_in_St._Louis" title="Meet Me in St. Louis">Meet Me in St. Louis</a></i> received an Academy Award nomination.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Stompin%27_Tom_Connors" title="Stompin' Tom Connors">Stompin' Tom Connors</a> song "To It And At It" mentions a man who "can't afford the train, he's sittin' on a streetcar, but he's eastbound just the same". And his song "TTC Skidaddler" makes reference to a TTC Streetcar driver: "I've been a streetcar driver now about eleven years and I know the old Toronto city well, There's a whole lotta people who wait along the track, For the signal from my clangin trolley bell...".</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jens_Lekman" title="Jens Lekman">Jens Lekman</a> has a song titled "Tram No. 7 to Heaven", a reference to line 7 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gothenburg_tram" title="Gothenburg tram">Gothenburg tram</a> which passes through his native borough of <a href="/wiki/Kortedala" title="Kortedala">Kortedala</a>.</li>
<li>The band <a href="/wiki/Beirut_(band)" title="Beirut (band)">Beirut</a> has a song titled "Fountains and Tramways" on the EP <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompeii_EP" title="Pompeii EP">Pompeii</a></i>.</li>
<li>In 2009 Thomas Haggerty composed and produced 'Tram' generations 1, 2 and 3 for the <a href="/wiki/Slowcore" title="Slowcore">Slowcore</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indie_Rock" title="Indie Rock">Indie Rock</a> group, <a href="/wiki/Tram_(band)" title="Tram (band)">Tram</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_visual_arts">In the visual arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: In the visual arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tramway_(arts_centre)" title="Tramway (arts centre)">Tramway</a> is a contemporary visual and performing arts venue located in the <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a>. Based in the former Coplawill <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Corporation_Tramways" title="Glasgow Corporation Tramways">Glasgow Corporation Tramways</a> depot in the <a href="/wiki/Pollokshields" title="Pollokshields">Pollokshields</a> area of the South Side, it consists of two performance spaces and two galleries, as well as offering facilities for community and artistic projects. It is claimed to be one of the leading venues of its type in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li>A major feature of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spencer_Street_railway_station,_Melbourne" title="Spencer Street railway station, Melbourne">Spencer Street railway station, Melbourne</a> from 1978 to 2005 was the giant <i>Cavalcade of Transport</i> mural, measuring 7 by 38 metres (23 by 125 ft). It was financed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria,_Australia" title="Victoria, Australia">Victorian</a> state government, and painted by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Freedman" title="Harold Freedman">Harold Freedman</a>. It features all forms of transport used in Victoria from 1835 to 1978, with trams featuring prominently. A horizontal column of trams shows the progression of vehicle design, with some dozens of trams being illustrated. In 2000, during a revamp and renaming of the station to <a href="/wiki/Southern_Cross_railway_station" title="Southern Cross railway station">Southern Cross railway station</a>, part of the mural was removed. It was taken down completely in 2005 and, after a cleaning, was in 2007 relocated to Spencer Outlet Centre, adjoining the railway station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>A sculpture of tram 1040, the last numbered of Melbourne's iconic <a href="/wiki/W-class_Melbourne_tram" title="W-class Melbourne tram">"W" class trams</a> was unveiled at the corner of Flinders and Spencer Streets, Melbourne, in October 2013. The sculpture is the work of local artist David Bell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> It can be viewed from a number of tram routes, and is just one block from <a href="/wiki/Southern_Cross_railway_station" title="Southern Cross railway station">Southern Cross railway station</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>A Melbourne tram is featured in an <a href="/wiki/Albert_Tucker_(artist)" title="Albert Tucker (artist)">Albert Tucker</a> painting in his 1945 series <i>Images of Modern Evil</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> The original is held in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Victoria" title="National Gallery of Victoria">National Gallery of Victoria</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_drama">In drama</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: In drama">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(play)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (play)"><i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i></a> was written by <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Williams" title="Tennessee Williams">Tennessee Williams</a> in 1947.</li>
<li>The Australian play, <i>Storming Mont Albert by Tram</i>, is set on <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_tram_route_109" title="Melbourne tram route 109">Melbourne tram route 109</a>. Written by Paul Davies, it was first performed in February/March 1982 as part of Melbourne's <a href="/wiki/Moomba" title="Moomba">Moomba</a> festival.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_film">In film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: In film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malcolmdvd.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="200" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9c/Malcolmdvd.jpg/170px-Malcolmdvd.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/9c/Malcolmdvd.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malcolmdvd.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The film <i>Malcolm</i> features a wayward tram enthusiast</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a> was a well-known rail enthusiast with a particular interest in <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_London" title="Trams in London">London trams</a>. An overwhelming majority of his films include rail and/or tram scenes, in particular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Lady_Vanishes_(1938_film)" title="The Lady Vanishes (1938 film)">The Lady Vanishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strangers_on_a_Train_(film)" title="Strangers on a Train (film)">Strangers on a Train</a> and <a href="/wiki/Number_Seventeen" title="Number Seventeen">Number Seventeen</a>. Often, when the <a href="/wiki/Scene_(filmmaking)" title="Scene (filmmaking)">scene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Take" title="Take">take</a> numbers appeared on a <a href="/wiki/Clapperboard" title="Clapperboard">clapperboard</a> during filming, Hitchcock would translate them into London tram route numbers; for example, if Scene 23, Take 2, appeared on the clapperboard, he was wont to whisper: "Woodford; Wimbledon" (the termini of Routes 23 and 2).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></li>
<li>Dziga Vertov's experimental 1929 film <a href="/wiki/Man_with_a_Movie_Camera" title="Man with a Movie Camera">Man with a Movie Camera</a> includes shots of trams (at 10 and 42 minutes).</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Black_Orpheus" title="Black Orpheus">Black Orpheus</a></i> (1959), has a lead character, Orfeu, who is a tram driver on <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>'s tram system.</li>
<li>The central plot of the film <i><a href="/wiki/Who_Framed_Roger_Rabbit" title="Who Framed Roger Rabbit">Who Framed Roger Rabbit</a></i> involves Judge Doom, the villain, dismantling the streetcars of Los Angeles.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Malcolm_(film)" title="Malcolm (film)"><i>Malcolm</i></a>, is an Australian film about a tram enthusiast who uses his inventions to pull off a bank heist. There are many scenes of <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Melbourne" title="Trams in Melbourne">Melbourne</a> trams, as well as models of <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Adelaide" title="Trams in Adelaide">Adelaide</a> trams and (at the end of the film) scenes showing <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_Lisbon" title="Trams in Lisbon">Lisbon trams</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Luis_Bu%C3%B1uel" title="Luis Buñuel">Luis Buñuel</a> filmed <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Ilusi%C3%B3n_viaja_en_tranv%C3%ADa" title="La Ilusión viaja en tranvía">La Ilusión viaja en tranvía</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> (English: <i><a href="/wiki/Illusion_Travels_by_Streetcar" title="Illusion Travels by Streetcar">Illusion Travels by Streetcar</a></i>) in Mexico in 1953.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Akira_Kurosawa" title="Akira Kurosawa">Akira Kurosawa</a>'s film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dodesukaden" title="Dodesukaden">Dodesukaden</a></i> a mentally ill boy pretends to be a tram conductor.</li>
<li><i>The Elephant Will Never Forget</i>, is an 11-minute film made in 1953 by <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Films" title="British Transport Films">British Transport Films</a> to celebrate the London tram network, at the time of its last few days of operation.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Tram_(film)" title="Tram (film)">Tramvaj</a> (</i>Tram<i>) is an eight-minute,2012 Czech short animated film directed by <a href="/wiki/Michaela_Pavl%C3%A1tov%C3%A1" title="Michaela Pavlátová">Michaela Pavlátová</a>.</i></li>
<li>The 1953 British film, <i><a href="/wiki/Genevieve" title="Genevieve">Genevieve</a></i> is about <a href="/wiki/London_to_Brighton_Veteran_Car_Run" title="London to Brighton Veteran Car Run">vintage cars</a> and, more particularly, the eccentricities of the car owners. The second part of the movie is about an unauthorised race between <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">Brighton</a> and <a href="/wiki/London_Bridge" title="London Bridge">London Bridge</a> by the film's two leads, played by <a href="/wiki/John_Gregson" title="John Gregson">John Gregson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_More" title="Kenneth More">Kenneth More</a>. After many trials and tribulations, the hero (Gregson) eventually wins the race when the wheels of More's car gets stuck in the <a href="/wiki/Trams_in_London" title="Trams in London">tram</a> tracks and he moves in entirely the wrong direction, just before reaching the Bridge.</li>
<li>There have been three film versions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(play)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (play)"><i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i></a>: in <a href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(1951_film)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (1951 film)">1951</a>, in <a href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(1984_film)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (1984 film)">1984</a> and in <a href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(1995_film)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (1995 film)">1995</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ballets">Ballets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Ballets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>The 1993 ballet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(ballet)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (ballet)">A Streetcar Named Desire</a> is based on the play <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(play)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (play)"><i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i></a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operas">Operas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Operas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>The 1995 opera <a href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(opera)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (opera)">A Streetcar Named Desire</a> is based on the play <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Streetcar_Named_Desire_(play)" title="A Streetcar Named Desire (play)"><i>A Streetcar Named Desire</i></a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_television">On television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: On television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>The US children's TV show <i><a href="/wiki/Mister_Rogers%27_Neighborhood" title="Mister Rogers' Neighborhood">Mister Rogers' Neighborhood</a></i> features a trolley (tram). It is shown on <a href="/wiki/National_Educational_Television" title="National Educational Television">National Educational Television</a>, <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sprout_(TV_network)" title="Sprout (TV network)">Sprout</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="Canadian Broadcasting Corporation">Canadian Broadcasting Corporation</a>.</li>
<li>Trams feature in the opening credits of the world's longest running TV soap opera <a href="/wiki/Coronation_Street" title="Coronation Street">Coronation Street</a>, set in a fictional suburb of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Manchester" title="Greater Manchester">Greater Manchester</a>, and produced by the UK's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Granada_Television" title="Granada Television">Granada Television</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Blackpool" title="Blackpool">Blackpool</a> tram killed one of the main characters in 1989 and the most recent faked accident involved a tram (modelled on the <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Metrolink" title="Manchester Metrolink">Manchester Metrolink</a>) careering off a viaduct into the set in 2009.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plate_commemorating_Moscow%27s_first_trams.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Plate_commemorating_Moscow%27s_first_trams.JPG/220px-Plate_commemorating_Moscow%27s_first_trams.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Plate_commemorating_Moscow%27s_first_trams.JPG/330px-Plate_commemorating_Moscow%27s_first_trams.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Plate_commemorating_Moscow%27s_first_trams.JPG/440px-Plate_commemorating_Moscow%27s_first_trams.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plate_commemorating_Moscow%27s_first_trams.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A lithographic transfer porcelain plate commemorating the launch of the first trams in Moscow and Nizhni Novgorod, in 1896-99. Decorated with a tram running on an overhead electric cable, the plate also shows a building with an onion-shaped dome. Designed by Vitaly Vlasoff. Imperial Porcelain Factory, St Petersburg, 2006.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Toonerville_Folks" title="Toonerville Folks">Toonerville Folks</a></i> comic strip (1908–55) by <a href="/wiki/Fontaine_Fox" title="Fontaine Fox">Fontaine Fox</a> featured the "Toonerville Trolley that met all the trains."</li>
<li>In US <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a>, the <a href="/wiki/1944_World_Series" title="1944 World Series">1944 World Series</a> was also known as the "Streetcar Series".</li>
<li>The predominance of trams (trolleys) in the borough of <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">Brooklyn</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> gave rise to the disparaging term <a href="/wiki/Trolley_dodger" title="Trolley dodger">trolley dodger</a> for residents of the borough. That term, shortened to "Dodger" became the nickname for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Dodgers" title="Brooklyn Dodgers">Brooklyn Dodgers</a> (now the Los Angeles Dodgers).</li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/W-class_Melbourne_tram" title="W-class Melbourne tram">W-class Melbourne tram</a> was used at the opening ceremony of the <a href="/wiki/2006_Commonwealth_Games" title="2006 Commonwealth Games">2006 Commonwealth Games</a> in Melbourne. The "flying tram" (as it was dubbed) is now exhibited at the <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_Museum" title="Melbourne Museum">Melbourne Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tramway,_North_Carolina" title="Tramway, North Carolina">Tramway, North Carolina</a>, is an area of <a href="/wiki/Lee_County,_North_Carolina" title="Lee County, North Carolina">Lee County, North Carolina</a> which politically forms part of <a href="/wiki/Sanford,_North_Carolina" title="Sanford, North Carolina">Sanford</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_incidents_involving_trams">Notable incidents involving trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Notable incidents involving trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>In March 1864, well-known Australian musician and composer <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Nathan" title="Isaac Nathan">Isaac Nathan</a> was hit and killed by a Sydney horse tram. Nathan is reputed to be the first tram fatality in the Southern Hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></li>
<li>On the morning of 18 August 1901, four masked men, described as "urban <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bushrangers" title="Bushrangers">bushrangers</a>", held up an eastbound horse tram in Riversdale Rd, <a href="/wiki/Hawthorn,_Victoria" title="Hawthorn, Victoria">Hawthorn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, just past Power St. For their trouble the men received <a href="/wiki/Australian_pound" title="Australian pound">£2.10.0</a> in fares from driver Thomas Taylor, and £21.19.0 from eight passengers. One passenger was injured. The bandits were never caught. Contemporary newspapers hypothesised that the bandits were after a specific commuter who travelled regularly on this particular tram and who was in the habit of carrying large amounts of cash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/Tottenham_Outrage" title="Tottenham Outrage">Tottenham Outrage</a> in 1909, two armed robbers hijacked a tram and were chased by the police in another tram.</li>
<li>On 7 June 1926 Catalan architect <a href="/wiki/Antoni_Gaud%C3%AD" title="Antoni Gaudí">Antoni Gaudí</a> was knocked down by a <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a> tram and subsequently died.</li>
<li>It is reputed that in the 1930s a murdered body was dragged out of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thames" title="Thames">Thames</a> River in London. The body had been stripped of anything that might have identified him. The only clue to the person's identity was a portion of a tram ticket hidden in the lining of his coat. The <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police_Service" title="Metropolitan Police Service">local police</a> did not recognise the ticket but images in newspapers led to it being identified as a <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_and_Metropolitan_Tramways_Board" title="Melbourne and Metropolitan Tramways Board">Melbourne tram</a> ticket. Serendipitously, the serial number on the ticket was intact. <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Police" title="Victoria Police">Victoria Police</a> in Melbourne, acting as agents for The Met in London, contacted the <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_and_Metropolitan_Tramways_Board" title="Melbourne and Metropolitan Tramways Board">Melbourne and Metropolitan Tramways Board</a>. From the serial number, the M&amp;MTB were able to tell which tram depot had issued the ticket, on what day and on which specific tram, and in which section of a particular route (<a href="/wiki/Melbourne_tram_route_48" title="Melbourne tram route 48">North Balwyn</a>). Police then interviewed regular commuters and discovered the identity of a man whom, they believed, had recently travelled to London. This led to the arrest and conviction of the murderer. Decades after the event, the M&amp;MTB were still citing the incident in training courses as a reason for tram conductors, etc., to keep proper and efficient records.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scale_modelling_of_trams">Scale modelling of trams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Scale modelling of trams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hobby_Model_Expo_2013_modello_ATM_5400.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1119" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hobby_Model_Expo_2013_modello_ATM_5400.JPG/220px-Hobby_Model_Expo_2013_modello_ATM_5400.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hobby_Model_Expo_2013_modello_ATM_5400.JPG/330px-Hobby_Model_Expo_2013_modello_ATM_5400.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hobby_Model_Expo_2013_modello_ATM_5400.JPG/440px-Hobby_Model_Expo_2013_modello_ATM_5400.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hobby_Model_Expo_2013_modello_ATM_5400.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An HO model PCC streetcar</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Model trams are popular in <a href="/wiki/HO_scale" title="HO scale">HO scale</a> (1:87) and <a href="/wiki/O_scale" title="O scale">O scale</a> (1:48 in the US and generally 1:43,5 and 1:45 in Europe and Asia). They are typically powered and will accept plastic figures inside. Common manufacturers are <a href="/wiki/Roco" title="Roco">Roco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lima_(models)" title="Lima (models)">Lima</a>, with many custom models being made as well. The German firm Hödl<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> and the Austrian Halling<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> specialize in 1:87 scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blackburn_and_East_Lancs_MRS_-_44th_annual_exhibition_-_DSC03803.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Blackburn_and_East_Lancs_MRS_-_44th_annual_exhibition_-_DSC03803.JPG/220px-Blackburn_and_East_Lancs_MRS_-_44th_annual_exhibition_-_DSC03803.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Blackburn_and_East_Lancs_MRS_-_44th_annual_exhibition_-_DSC03803.JPG/330px-Blackburn_and_East_Lancs_MRS_-_44th_annual_exhibition_-_DSC03803.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Blackburn_and_East_Lancs_MRS_-_44th_annual_exhibition_-_DSC03803.JPG/440px-Blackburn_and_East_Lancs_MRS_-_44th_annual_exhibition_-_DSC03803.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blackburn_and_East_Lancs_MRS_-_44th_annual_exhibition_-_DSC03803.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A model tramway</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the US, <a href="/wiki/Bachmann_Industries" title="Bachmann Industries">Bachmann Industries</a> is a mass supplier of HO streetcars and kits. <a href="/wiki/Bowser_Manufacturing" title="Bowser Manufacturing">Bowser Manufacturing</a> has produced white metal models for over 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> There are many boutique vendors offering limited run epoxy and wood models. At the high end are highly detailed brass models which are usually imported from Japan or Korea and can cost in excess of $500. Many of these run on <span class="nowrap">16.5 mm</span> (<span class="nowrap">0.65 in</span>) gauge track, which is correct for the representation of <span class="nowrap">4 ft <span class="frac">8<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> in</span> (<span class="nowrap">1,435 mm</span>) (standard gauge) in HO scale as in US and Japan, but incorrect in 4 mm (1:76.2) scale, as it represents <span class="nowrap">4 ft <span class="frac">8<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> in</span> (<span class="nowrap">1,435 mm</span>). This scale/gauge hybrid is called OO scale. O scale trams are also very popular among tram modellers because the increased size allows for more detail and easier crafting of overhead wiring. In the US these models are usually purchased in epoxy or wood kits and some as brass models. The Saint Petersburg Tram Company<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> produces highly detailed polyurethane non-powered O Scale models from around the world which can easily be powered by trucks from vendors like Q-Car.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the US, one of the best resources for model tram enthusiasts is the East Penn Traction Club of Philadelphia <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> and Trolleyville a website of the Southern California Traction Club.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is thought that the first example of a working model tramcar in the UK built by an amateur for fun was in 1929, when Frank E. Wilson created a replica of London County Council Tramways E class car 444 in 1:16 scale, which he demonstrated at an early Model Engineer Exhibition. Another of his models was London E/1 1800, which was the only tramway exhibit in the Faraday Memorial Exhibition of 1931. Together with likeminded friends, Frank Wilson went on to found the Tramway &amp; Light Railway Society<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> in 1938, establishing tramway modelling as a hobby.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<ul>
<li>Arrivetz, Jean. 1956. "Les Tramways Français" (No ISBN). Lyon: Editions Omni-Presse.</li>
<li>Bett, W. C., and J. C. Gillam. 1962. "Great British Tramway Networks (4th Edition)", <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0900433035">ISBN 0-900433-03-5</a>. London: <a href="/wiki/Light_Rail_Transit_Association" title="Light Rail Transit Association">Light Railway Transport League</a>.</li>
<li>Bigon, Liora. 2007, "Tracking Ethno- Cultural Differences: The Lagos Steam Tramway (1902-1933)" Journal of Historical Geography, 33, 3</li>
<li>Brimson, Samuel. 1983. "The Tramways of Australia" (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0949825018">ISBN 0-949825-01-8</a>). Sydney: Dreamweaver Books.</li>
<li>Buckley, R. J. 1984. "Tramways and Light Railways of Switzerland and Austria" (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0900433965">ISBN 0-900433-96-5</a>). Milton Keynes, UK: <a href="/wiki/Light_Rail_Transit_Association" title="Light Rail Transit Association">Light Rail Transit Association</a>.</li>
<li>Chandler, Allison. 1963. "Trolley Through the Countryside" (No ISBN). Denver: Sage Books.</li>
<li>Cheape, Charles W. <i>Moving the masses: urban public transit in New York, Boston, and Philadelphia, 1880-1912</i> (Harvard University Press, 1980)</li>
<li>Davies, W. K. J. 1986. "100 years of the Belgian vicinal: SNCV/NMVB, 1885–1985 : a century of secondary rail transport in Belgium" (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0900433973">ISBN 0-900433-97-3</a>). Broxbourne, UK: Light Rail Transit Association.</li>
<li>Dunbar, Charles S. 1967. "Buses, Trolleys &amp; Trams" Great Britain: Paul Hamlyn Ltd. [republished 2004 with <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0753709708">ISBN 0-7537-0970-8</a> or 9780753709702]</li>
<li>Dyer, Peter, and Peter Hodge. 1988. "Cane Train: The Sugar-Cane Railways of Fiji" (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0908573502">ISBN 0-908573-50-2</a>). Wellington: New Zealand Railway and Locomotive Society Inc.</li>
<li>Gragt, Frits van der. 1968. "Europe's Greatest Tramway Network" (No ISBN). Leiden, Netherlands: E.J. Brill.</li>
<li>Hilton, George W. 1997. "The Cable Car in America: A New Treatise upon Cable or Rope Traction As Applied to the Working of Street and Other Railways", Revised Edition (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0804730512">ISBN 0-8047-3051-2</a>). Stanford (CA), US: Stanford University Press.</li>
<li>Howarth, W. Des. 1971. "Tramway Systems of Southern Africa" (No ISBN). Johannesburg: published by the author.</li>
<li>King, B. R., and J. H. Price. 1995. "The Tramways of Portugal (4th Edition)" (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0948106190">ISBN 0-948106-19-0</a>). London: <a href="/wiki/Light_Rail_Transit_Association" title="Light Rail Transit Association">Light Rail Transit Association</a>.</li>
<li>McKay, John P. <i>Tramways and Trolleys: The Rise of Urban Mass Transport in Europe</i> (1976)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_D._Middleton" title="William D. Middleton">Middleton, William D.</a> 1967. <i>The Time of the Trolley</i> (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0890240132">ISBN 0-89024-013-2</a>). Milwaukee (WI), US: <a href="/wiki/Kalmbach_Publishing" title="Kalmbach Publishing">Kalmbach Publishing</a>.</li>
<li>Morrison, Allen. 1989. <a class="external text" href="http://www.tramz.com/br/tto/01.html" rel="nofollow">"The Tramways of Brazil – A 130-Year Survey"</a> (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0962234818">ISBN 0-9622348-1-8</a>). New York: Bonde Press.</li>
<li>Morrison, Allen. 1992. <a class="external text" href="http://www.tramz.com/cl/tto/a.html" rel="nofollow">"The Tramways of Chile – 1858–1978"</a> (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0962234826">ISBN 0-9622348-2-6</a>). New York: Bonde Press.</li>
<li>Morrison, Allen. 1996. "Latin America by Streetcar: A Pictorial Survey of Urban Rail Transport South of the U.S.A." (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0962234834">ISBN 0-9622348-3-4</a>). New York: Bonde Press.</li>
<li>Nye, David E.: <i>Electrifying America : social meanings of a new technology, 1880–1940</i>, MIT Press, Cambridge, Massachusetts c1990. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0262140489">ISBN 0-262-14048-9</a></li>
<li>Pabst, Martin. 1989. "Tram &amp; Trolley in Africa" (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3884901524">ISBN 3-88490-152-4</a>). Krefeld: Röhr Verlag GMBH.</li>
